Abu Hashim's First Proof: The Column and Support
First proof: column and support example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hashim's first proof demonstrates that i'timad generates motion and rest, using the example of a supported column.

Core Idea
Motion can arise from pressure (i'timad) without prior motion.

Abu Hashim argues that the cause of motion and rest is i'timad, not motion itself. His first proof uses a concrete physical example: a column that can stand on its own, a support, and a leaning object. By observing what happens when the support is removed, he shows that the column's fall is caused by the i'timad of the leaning object, not by any prior motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first proof and its translation. Notice the setup: a column, a support, and a leaning object.
The passage uses the terms عمود (column) and دعامة (support).
2
Identify the logic
Follow the sequence: the column does not move when the support is present, even with the leaning object pushing. When the support is removed, the column falls toward the support.
The key is that the leaning object does not move further, so motion is not the cause.
3
Understand the conclusion
The column's motion (falling) is generated by the i'timad of the leaning object, not by any motion. This supports the claim that i'timad generates motion and rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابنه: المولد لهما أي للحركة والسكون هو الاعتماد لا الحركة لوجهين: الأول: أنه إذا أقيم عمود يمكن انتصابه قائماً على رأسه منفرداً فنصب كذلك وأدعم بدعامة ثم اعتمد عليه معتمد إلى جهة الدعامة لم يتحرك ذلك العمود إلى تلك الجهة فإن الدعامة تمنعه ذلك، ثم إذا أزيلت دعامته سقط إلى جهة الدعامة وإن لم يتحرك ذلك المعتمد إلى جهتها فعلمنا أن حركة العمود لم تتولد من الحركة بل من الاعتماد. الثاني: حركة اليد متأخرة عن حركة الحجر إذ ما لم يتحرك مكانه امتنع حركة اليد إليه لامتناع التداخل بين الأجسام، والمتأخر لا يولد المتقدم وفيه نظر إذ لا تأخر هناك بحسب الزمان بل هما معاً بحسبه فلا يلزم التداخل، وأما بحسب الذات فحركة اليد متقدمة إذ يصح أن يقال: تحركت اليد فتحرك الحجر ولا عكسه فجاز أن تكون حركة اليد مولدة لحركة الحجر.
And his son (Abu Hashim) said: The generator of both (motion and rest) is i'timad, not motion, for two reasons: First: if one erects a column that can stand upright on its own, and sets it up thus, and supports it with a support, and then a leaning object leans on it toward the direction of the support, that column does not move in that direction because the support prevents it. Then when its support is removed, it falls toward the direction of the support, even if the leaning object has not moved toward that direction. So we know that the column's movement was not generated from motion but from i'timad. Second: the hand's movement is posterior to the stone's movement; for as long as the stone's place has not moved, the hand's movement to it is impossible due to the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies. The posterior does not generate the prior. But there is a consideration: there is no temporal posteriority; both are simultaneous in time, so interpenetration does not follow. However, in terms of essence, the hand's movement is prior, for it is correct to say 'the hand moved, so the stone moved,' and not the reverse. So it is possible that the hand's movement generates the stone's movement.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim's (son of al-Jubba'i) view that i'timad generates both motion and rest. He gives two proofs. First proof: A column is set upright and supported; a leaning object leans toward the support, but the column does not move because the support prevents it. When the support is removed, the column falls toward the support even without the leaning object moving further. This shows that the column's motion (falling) is generated by the i'timad of the leaning object, not by any prior motion. Second proof: When a hand moves a stone, the hand's movement is temporally simultaneous with the stone's movement, so the hand's movement cannot generate the stone's due to the impossibility of interpenetration if one preceded. However, the commentator notes that temporal posteriority does not actually exist; they are simultaneous. In terms of essential priority, the hand's movement is prior (we say 'the hand moved, so the stone moved'), so it could generate movement. Thus Abu Hashim's second proof is criticised. The passage thus presents Abu Hashim's two arguments and a refutation of the second.

Key Terms
العمود (al-ʿamūd): column
الدعامة (al-diʿāmah): support
الاعتماد (al-iʿtimād): leaning/pressure

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Abu Hashim's first proof, what happens to the column when the support is removed?
A) It remains stationary
B) It falls toward the support
C) It falls away from the support
D) It moves in the direction of the leaning object

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's first proof shows that i'timad, not motion, generates motion in the column.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you felt 'supported' by something or someone, and when that support was removed, you 'fell' in a certain direction. What was the 'i'timad'—the hidden pressure or leaning—that determined that direction? Was it your attachment to that support, or something else? How does this lesson reveal that your movements may stem from unseen pressures, not just visible motions?
?
Abu Hashim's proof isolates i'timad as the cause of motion, even when the leaning object doesn't move. What 'leaning' in your heart is determining your actions, even when you feel still? Are you aware of the unseen pressures—like desires, fears, or habits—that are moving you? This lesson asks: what is the real i'timad that controls your direction?
?
The column fell toward the direction of the leaning object. In your spiritual life, what is your deepest leaning—toward Allah or toward created things? When the support of worldly pleasures or human approval is removed, which way do you fall? This proof challenges you to identify the true source of your spiritual motion.

Abu Hashim's Second Proof: Hand and Stone, and Its Critique
Second proof and its criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's second proof that i'timad generates motion, and why it is criticized on temporal and essential grounds.

Core Idea
Temporal simultaneity undermines the second proof, but essential priority might save it.

Abu Hashim's second proof argues that the hand's movement cannot generate the stone's movement because the hand moves after the stone, making it a posterior cause—which is impossible. However, the commentator points out that the hand and stone move simultaneously in time, so the fallacy of 'posterior generating prior' does not apply. Yet in terms of essential order, the hand's movement is prior, leaving the possibility that motion can generate motion after all.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second proof. Note the claim: the hand's movement is posterior to the stone's movement.
The term متأخر (posterior) and متقدم (prior) are key.
2
Understand the objection
Abu Hashim argues that if the hand moves after the stone, the hand cannot generate the stone's motion (the posterior cannot generate the prior).
3
Follow the critique
The commentator rejects the temporal posteriority: the hand and stone move simultaneously. But in essence, the hand is prior (we say 'the hand moved, so the stone moved'). This reopens the possibility that motion generates motion.
The critique does not fully destroy the second proof but weakens it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابنه: المولد لهما أي للحركة والسكون هو الاعتماد لا الحركة لوجهين: الأول: أنه إذا أقيم عمود يمكن انتصابه قائماً على رأسه منفرداً فنصب كذلك وأدعم بدعامة ثم اعتمد عليه معتمد إلى جهة الدعامة لم يتحرك ذلك العمود إلى تلك الجهة فإن الدعامة تمنعه ذلك، ثم إذا أزيلت دعامته سقط إلى جهة الدعامة وإن لم يتحرك ذلك المعتمد إلى جهتها فعلمنا أن حركة العمود لم تتولد من الحركة بل من الاعتماد. الثاني: حركة اليد متأخرة عن حركة الحجر إذ ما لم يتحرك مكانه امتنع حركة اليد إليه لامتناع التداخل بين الأجسام، والمتأخر لا يولد المتقدم وفيه نظر إذ لا تأخر هناك بحسب الزمان بل هما معاً بحسبه فلا يلزم التداخل، وأما بحسب الذات فحركة اليد متقدمة إذ يصح أن يقال: تحركت اليد فتحرك الحجر ولا عكسه فجاز أن تكون حركة اليد مولدة لحركة الحجر.
And his son (Abu Hashim) said: The generator of both (motion and rest) is i'timad, not motion, for two reasons: First: if one erects a column that can stand upright on its own, and sets it up thus, and supports it with a support, and then a leaning object leans on it toward the direction of the support, that column does not move in that direction because the support prevents it. Then when its support is removed, it falls toward the direction of the support, even if the leaning object has not moved toward that direction. So we know that the column's movement was not generated from motion but from i'timad. Second: the hand's movement is posterior to the stone's movement; for as long as the stone's place has not moved, the hand's movement to it is impossible due to the impossibility of interpenetration of bodies. The posterior does not generate the prior. But there is a consideration: there is no temporal posteriority; both are simultaneous in time, so interpenetration does not follow. However, in terms of essence, the hand's movement is prior, for it is correct to say 'the hand moved, so the stone moved,' and not the reverse. So it is possible that the hand's movement generates the stone's movement.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim's (son of al-Jubba'i) view that i'timad generates both motion and rest. He gives two proofs. First proof: A column is set upright and supported; a leaning object leans toward the support, but the column does not move because the support prevents it. When the support is removed, the column falls toward the support even without the leaning object moving further. This shows that the column's motion (falling) is generated by the i'timad of the leaning object, not by any prior motion. Second proof: When a hand moves a stone, the hand's movement is temporally simultaneous with the stone's movement, so the hand's movement cannot generate the stone's due to the impossibility of interpenetration if one preceded. However, the commentator notes that temporal posteriority does not actually exist; they are simultaneous. In terms of essential priority, the hand's movement is prior (we say 'the hand moved, so the stone moved'), so it could generate movement. Thus Abu Hashim's second proof is criticised. The passage thus presents Abu Hashim's two arguments and a refutation of the second.

Key Terms
التداخل (al-tadākhul): interpenetration
المتقدم (al-mutaqaddim): the prior
المتأخر (al-mutaʾakhkhir): the posterior

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the hand moves after the stone, it cannot cause the stone's motion, but the critique shows temporal simultaneity avoids this.
Correction: The hand and stone move simultaneously in time, so the posterior-before-prior argument does not apply.

Checkpoint
According to the critique of Abu Hashim's second proof, why does the temporal argument fail?
A) The hand moves before the stone
B) The hand and stone move simultaneously
C) The stone moves before the hand
D) Interpenetration is possible

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's second proof is weakened by the observation that the hand and stone move simultaneously, but essential priority leaves the door open for motion generating motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because two events happen at the same time, one cannot be caused by the other? How does this lesson's distinction between temporal and essential priority force you to re-evaluate your everyday assumptions about cause and effect?
?
Consider the phrase 'the hand moved, so the stone moved.' In your daily life, do you habitually see created beings as independent agents, or do you acknowledge that their actions are essentially preceded by Allah's power? What would change if you truly lived by the insight that essential priority, not temporal sequence, is the real mark of causation?

Ibn 'Ayyash's Mediation on Generation
Ibn 'Ayyash allows generation from both motion and i'timad; commentator rejects the theory of generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn 'Ayyash's mediating position on the generation of motion and rest, and how the commentator subjects the entire generative theory to critique.

Core Idea
Ibn 'Ayyash allows generation from both motion and i'timad, but the theory itself is ultimately false.

Ibn 'Ayyash, a Basran theologian, attempts to reconcile the views of al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim on the generation of motion and rest. He argues that since neither side's evidence proves exclusivity, we should allow generation from both motion and i'timad. However, the commentator reminds us that the entire theory of generation (tawallud) is false—a crucial metacritique that frames the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Note the key terms: mutamassak (evidence), inḥiṣār (restriction), tawallud (generation).
The passage is slightly fragmented due to OCR; focus on the logical structure.
2
Identify Ibn 'Ayyash's position
Identify why Ibn 'Ayyash allows generation from both motion and i'timad. He points out that each school's evidence indicates generation from its source without proving exclusivity.
The evidence of al-Jubba'i shows generation from motion; the evidence of his son (Abu Hashim) shows generation from i'timad.
3
Understand the commentator's critique
The commentator states that the whole theory of generation is false, so Ibn 'Ayyash's argument, built on that theory, is also false. Then al-Amidi concedes the theory for the sake of argument and critiques al-Jubba'i's specific claim.
This shows a layered approach: first a synthetic view, then a meta-critique.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why the commentator would reject the entire generative framework. How does this impact the value of Ibn 'Ayyash's mediation?
This lesson connects to broader discussions in Islamic theology about causality and the rejection of secondary causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابن عياش من البصريين بتولدهما أي بتولد الحركة والسكون من الحركة تارة ومن الاعتماد أخرى لمتمسكيهما، فإن متمسك الجبائي دل على تولدهما من الحركة من غير دلالة على انحصار تولدهما فيها، ومتمسك ابنه دل على تولد الحركة والسكون من الاعتماد بلا دلالة على الانحصار، فالصواب حينئذ تجويز تولدهما من كل واحد من الاعتماد والحركة. ولما كان القول بالتوليد باطلاً كما ستعرفه كان هذا الكلام المبني عليه باطلاً أيضاً، لكن الآمدي تنزل إلى صحة التوليد ثم ناقضهم فقال على الجبائي: كما أن حركة المفتاح متعقبة لحركة اليد كذلك هي متعقبة لاعتماد...
And Ibn 'Ayyash from the Basrans held that both (motion and rest) are generated—i.e., motion and rest are generated—sometimes from motion and sometimes from i'timad, due to the evidence for each side. For the evidence of al-Jubba'i indicates their generation from motion without indicating that their generation is restricted to it, and the evidence of his son indicates the generation of motion and rest from i'timad without indicating restriction. Therefore the correct view is to allow their generation from each of i'timad and motion. And since the doctrine of generation is false, as you will know, this statement built upon it is also false. However, al-Amidi conceded the validity of generation and then contradicted them, saying against al-Jubba'i: just as the movement of the key follows the hand's movement, so it follows i'timad...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage introduces Ibn 'Ayyash's mediating position: he accepts that motion and rest can be generated from either motion or i'timad, because each school (al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim) has evidence that generation occurs from one source but not exclusive evidence. So the correct view is to allow both possibilities. The commentator then steps back and notes that the entire theory of generation (tawallud) is ultimately false (as will be explained later). Nevertheless, al-Amidi (the commentator) provisionally accepts generation and then critiques al-Jubba'i's specific argument: the key's movement follows the hand's movement, but it also follows i'timad (since the hand has i'timad). The passage thus presents a synthetic view and a meta-critique of the generative framework.

Key Terms
ابن عياش (Ibn ʿAyyāsh): a Basran theologian
متمسك (mutamassak): evidence, proof
الانحصار (al-inḥiṣār): restriction, exclusivity
التوليد (al-tawallud): generation
الآمدي (al-Āmidī): the commentator

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn 'Ayyash's view is the final correct position in the debate.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that the theory of generation is false, so Ibn 'Ayyash's argument is also false. The passage uses Ibn 'Ayyash's view to illustrate a flawed framework.

Checkpoint
What is Ibn 'Ayyash's position on whether motion and rest are generated?
A) Only motion can generate
B) Only i'timad can generate
C) Both motion and i'timad can generate
D) Neither can generate

Key Takeaway
Ibn 'Ayyash attempts to reconcile the two Basran views on generation by allowing both sources, but the commentator ultimately rejects the entire theory of generation as false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn 'Ayyash tried to reconcile two flawed evidences by allowing both sources of generation, but the commentator rejected the entire theory as false. In your own life, when have you tried to reconcile ideas or habits that were built on a false foundation? What would it mean to let go of the entire framework rather than just adjusting it?
?
The passage shows that even respected theologians like al-Jubba'i had arguments that were incomplete (they did not prove exclusivity). Are you willing to admit that some of your deeply held beliefs might be based on incomplete evidence? Can you take one belief this week and examine whether your evidence for it is truly exclusive?
?
Al-Amidi provisionally accepted the theory of generation in order to critique it. Think of a mistaken worldview or sin that you have 'conceded' to yourself in order to argue against it—only to find yourself drawn into it. Are you giving ground to falsehood by engaging it on its own terms? How can you stand on truth without capitulating to error?

The Generation of Downward Motion: Al-Jubba'i vs. His Son
Debate on generation of motion in a thrown stone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate on the generation of motion applies to a concrete example (a stone thrown upward) and why the author considers both positions lacking a decisive proof.

Core Idea
In kalām, even a falling stone reveals deep disagreements on causality.

This passage presents a specific case study from the debate on the generation (tawlid) of motion: a stone thrown upward by compulsion, which then falls back down. Al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim disagree on whether its downward motion is generated from the preceding upward motion or from a descending reliance. The author then critiques both views as arbitrary. Reading this will clarify how abstract principles play out in concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: Al-Jubba'i's view, his son's view, then the author's comment.
The passage is cut off; we only have the beginning of the author's explanation.
2
Identify the two opposing principles
Al-Jubba'i says motion generates only from motion; his son says motion generates only from reliance (i'timad). See how each applies this to the stone.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author calls both views 'arbitrary judgment and weighting without preponderance'. Ask: why does he say that? What is missing?
This reveals the kalām standard of demanding a preponderant factor.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider why this debate matters: it shows that even everyday phenomena can be interpreted differently based on metaphysical commitments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( ومنها أنه قال : ( الجبائي ( في الحجر المرمي ) بالقسر ( إلى فوق إذا عاد هاوياً) أي نازلاً: أن حركته الهابطة متولدة من حركته الصاعدة ) بناء على أصله من أن الحركة إنما تتولد من الحركة لا من الاعتماد ) وقال ابنه : بل هي متولدة ( من الاعتماد الهابط ) ) الذي في الحجر بناء على أصله من أن الحركة إنما تتولد من الاعتماد لا من الحركة فلذلك قال المصنف : ( وهذا) فرع الخلاف الذي قبله ثم قال : ( وعلى الرأيين فيه تحكم وترجيح بلا مرجح ( أما الأول فلأنه إذا قيل : كل (حركة) من الحركات المتعاقبة
(And among them is that he said: (Al-Jubba'i (concerning the stone thrown) by compulsion (upward, when it returns descending) meaning falling: that its descending motion is generated from its ascending motion) based on his principle that motion is generated only from motion, not from reliance. And his son said: Rather, it is generated (from the descending reliance)) which is in the stone, based on his principle that motion is generated only from reliance, not from motion. Therefore, the author said: (And this) is a branch of the disagreement before it. Then he said: (And upon both views there is arbitrary judgment and weighting without preponderance. (As for the first, because if it is said: every (motion) of successive motions...
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a specific case in the debate on generation (tawlid) of motion. It cites the example of a stone thrown upward by compulsion (qasr) that then falls back down. Al-Jubba'i argues that the stone's downward motion is generated from its upward motion, adhering to his principle that motion can only be generated from motion, not from reliance (i'timad). His son Abu Hashim counters that the downward motion is generated from the downward reliance (i'timad hadit) inherent in the stone, following his own principle that motion is generated solely from reliance. The author (al-Iji) then comments that this case is merely a branch of the earlier disagreement on the general principle. He declares that both positions involve arbitrary judgment (tahakkum) and weighting without a preponderant factor (tarjih bila murajjih), indicating that neither side has a decisive proof for their exclusive claim. The passage ends with the beginning of an explanation of the first point ('As for the first, because if it is said: every motion of successive motions...').

Key Terms
اعتماد (i'timad)
توليد (tawlid)
حركة (haraka)
قسر (qasr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage describes a physical experiment or observable causality.
Correction: It is a metaphysical kalām argument about the generation of accidents (motion) and principles of causality, not empirical physics.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis of Al-Jubba'i's claim that the downward motion of the stone is generated from its upward motion?
A) His principle that motion is generated only from motion
B) His principle that motion is generated only from reliance
C) Direct observation of the stone's fall
D) The consensus of philosophers

Key Takeaway
The author presents both positions on the stone's fall as arbitrary, highlighting the need for a preponderant factor in kalām arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jubba'i and his son each held strong positions based on their principles, yet the author says both are arbitrary. Are there core beliefs in your faith that you hold simply because you inherited them or because of loyalty to a scholar, without examining whether they have a clear proof? What would it take for you to honestly reassess one such belief this week?
?
The phrase 'weighting without a preponderant factor' (tarjih bila murajjih) exposes how we sometimes favor one view over another without solid grounds. Can you identify a situation in your daily life—perhaps in how you practice a religious obligation or interact with others—where you have strongly preferred a course of action without a compelling reason? How will you seek the missing preponderant factor?
?
If both sides in a theological debate can be arbitrary, how do you ensure that your own understanding of causality and God's actions is not based on mere assumption? What concrete step will you take today to root your creed more firmly in evidence from revelation and sound reason?

The Debate on Motion Generation: Motion vs. Reliance
Al-Jurjani presents the debate between al-Jubba'i and his son on whether motion or reliance generates motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core disagreement between al-Jubba'i and his son on the principle of generation (tawlīd) and how each uses examples to argue whether motion or reliance is the true cause of generated motion.

Core Idea
The debate over generation hinges on whether motion alone suffices or requires the principle of reliance.

This lesson presents a classic debate in the Basran Mu'tazila tradition between al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim. The question is: when one object moves another, is the generated motion caused by the motion itself or by an underlying inclination (i‘timād)? Through examples of a hand moving a key and a stone thrown upward, both sides present their principles. Understanding this debate is crucial for grasping the role of causality in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the two opposing principles stated explicitly by al-Jubba'i and his son.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the argument.
2
Identify the two principles
Find the statements where al-Jubba'i says motion is independent in generation, and where his son says reliance is the source. Note how each principle is applied to the examples.
Look for phrases like 'بناء على أصله' (based on his principle).
3
Analyze the examples
Consider the hand-key example and the stone example. How does each side use these examples to support their principle? Then note the author's criticism that both views involve arbitrary assignment.
The author (al-Iji) calls both views 'taḥakkum' and 'tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ'.

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل اليد، فليس القول بتولدها عن حركة اليد بأولى من القول بتولدها من اعتماد اليد فإن قال الجبائي : قد استقلت الحركة بالتوليد في صورة وهي أن من حرك يده كانت حركة يده صادرة عنه مباشرة بالقدرة غير متولدة من شيء ويتولد من حركة يده حركة ما عليها من الشعر والأظفار، وحينئذ كان إسناد حركة المفتاح إلى حركة اليد أولى من إسنادها إلى اعتماد اليد قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن تكون حركة الشعر والأظفار متولدة من اعتماد اليد ومدافعتها لما عليها بسبب اتصالها بها فلا يثبت حينئذ استقلال الحركة بالتوليد ؟ وقال علي أبي هاشم لا أن نسلم حركة العمود بدون حركة المعتمد الدافع له، فلا نسلم أن حركة اليد لا تكون إلا بعد حركة الحجر، بل هما معاً في الزمان مع كون حركة الحجر مترتبة على حركة اليد كما مر تحقيقه . ( ومنها أنه قال : ( الجبائي ( في الحجر المرمي ) بالقسر ( إلى فوق إذا عاد هاوياً) أي نازلاً: أن حركته الهابطة متولدة من حركته الصاعدة ) بناء على أصله من أن الحركة إنما تتولد من الحركة لا من الاعتماد ) وقال ابنه : بل هي متولدة ( من الاعتماد الهابط ) ) الذي في الحجر بناء على أصله من أن الحركة إنما تتولد من الاعتماد لا من الحركة فلذلك قال المصنف : ( وهذا) فرع الخلاف الذي قبله ثم قال : ( وعلى الرأيين فيه تحكم وترجيح بلا مرجح ( أما الأول فلأنه إذا قيل : كل (حركة) من الحركات المتعاقبة قوله : ( ويتولد من حركة يده إلخ) لأنّ التوليد عبارة عن أن يوجب فعل لعامله فعه فعلاً آخر وليس هاهنا حركتان إحداهما حركة اليد وثانيتهما حركة الشعر والأظفار بل هي حركة واحدة تنسب إلى اليد بالذات وإلى ما عليها بالتبع كحركة راكب السفينة . قوله : ( كما مر تحقيقه بقوله : وفيه نظر ولما كان ما سبق منعاً للزوم التداخل وما ذكره الآمدي منعاً للبعدية لم يلزم التكرار . قوله: (فقال علي الجبائي إلخ) فإن قلت لعل الجبائي يتشبث بتوليد حركة جالس السفينة على لوح أملس منها من حركتها إذ لا اعتماد للسفينة على الجالس فتعين تولد حركته من حركتها . قلت : المراد إبطال ما ذكره في مثال حركة اليد والمفتاح فإن مدعى الجبائي تولد جميع الحركات المتولدة من الحركة فالإبطال في صورة إبطال لمدعاه وليس من قبيل المناقشة في المثال المردود عند المحققين. نعم للجبائي أن يجعل هذا من صور استقلال الحركة بالتوليد اللهم إلا أن يقال : المولد هاهنا أيضاً اعتماد الجالس على السفينة على أن الشارح لما صرح باعتماد اليد على ما عليها من الشعر والأظفار لم يبعد دعوى اعتماد السفينة على الجالس فيها . قوله : ( ويتولد من حركة يده حركة ما عليها من الشعر والأظفار إذ الشعر والأظفار لا حياة فيها فلا يتعدى إليها حكم القدرة حتى تكون متحركة بالقدرة مباشرة . في الصعود الثابتة للحجر المقسور ) ولدت حركة صاعدة إلا الحركة ( الأخيرة فإنها تولد ) حركة ( هابطة فهو تحكم ( بحت بل كان يجب أن يذهب ) الحجر المقسور ) إلى غير النهاية ) بأن يتولد من كل حركة من حركاته الصاعدة حركة أخرى صاعدة بل انقطاع ( وأما الثاني فلان (الاعتماد الهابط الذي في الحجر (إذا كان يوجب النزول فليوجبه (أولاً) أي في ابتداء الحركة، وأيضاً القول بأن كلاً اعتماداً صاعداً يوجب دون الاعتماد الأخير منها ترجيح بلا مرجح ( هكذا المجتلبة من الاعتمادات قيل في الاعتراض على الرأيين ) وفيه نظر لأن الحركة القسريه ( تضعف كلما بعدت عن المبدأ ) القاسر بسبب مقاومة الطبيعة لها منضمة إلى مقاومة ما في المسافة من الهواء الذي يحتاج المتحرك إلى خرقه ( فليست طبقاتها متماثلة) حتى يجب تساويها في الأحكام ( فقد تنتهي ( الحركة الصاعدة في الضعف (إلى ما يوجب ) أي إلى طبقة توجب الحركة ( النازلة ) التي هي ضدها دون الصاعدة التي هي مثلها، فإنَّ الشيء لا يؤثر في مثله إلا إذا كان قويا في الغاية وقد يؤثر في ضده مع ضعفه فاندفع التحكم عن الجبائي ( والاعتماد اللازم الذي في الحجر ( مغلوب في الأول ) أي في ابتداء الحركة ( بالمجتلب ) الذي أفاده القاسر ( ثم يضعف المجتلب قليلا قليلا بمقاومة الطبيعة والمخروق في دفعه ( حتى يصير) المجتلب ( مغلوباً) واللازم غالباً ( وحينئذ يوجب ) الاعتماد اللازم ( النزول ) . والجواب عن توليد الاعتمادات : ما مر في توليد الحركات، فاندفع التحكم عن ابنه أيضاً ، ( ومنها أنه قال أكثر المعتزلة: ليس بين الحركة الصاعدة والهابطة سكون إذ لا يوجبه الاعتماد لا اللازم فإنه يوجب الحركة الهابطة ) ولا المجتلب ) لأنه يقتضي الحركة الصاعدة فلا يتولد السكون قوله : (إذا قيل : كل حركة من الحركات المتعاقبة والصعود الحركة عند المتكلمين كونان في آنين في مكانين أو السكون الأول في المكان الثاني وعلى هذا لا شبهة في تعدد الحركات المتصاعدة في الصورة المفروضة وإن لم يتعدد عند الحكماء لا الحركة بمعنى التوسط ولا بمعنى القطع كما علم من قواعدهم. قوله : (منضمة إلى مقاومة ما في المسافة إلخ) هذا الانضمام أكثري وليس بلازم وإنما يلزم لو امتنع الخلاء في مسافة الحركة الصاعدة ولا امتناع عند المتكلمين ولو سلم امتناع الخلاء فإنما يلزم ما ذكر لو كان الهواء راكداً أو متحركاً إلى خلاف جهة الحركة إليها لكن مخالفاً للاعتمادات المجتلبة إذ لو قدر حركته إلى جهة العلو موافقاً في اعتماده للاعتمادات المجتلبة لم يوجد مقاومة ما في المسافة أيضاً.
The Third Observatory - The Third Intent: On Reliance, which is inclination. The hand: so the statement that its motion is generated from the motion of the hand is not more likely than the statement that it is generated from the reliance of the hand. If al-Jubba'i says: 'Motion has been independent in generation in one case, namely that when one moves his hand, the motion of his hand proceeds from him directly by power without being generated from anything, and from his hand's motion is generated the motion of what is upon it of hair and nails. Then attributing the motion of the key to the motion of the hand is more likely than attributing it to the reliance of the hand.' We say: 'Why is it not permissible that the motion of the hair and nails be generated from the reliance of the hand and its pushing against what is upon it due to its connection to them? Then the independence of motion in generation would not be established.' And Abu Hashim said: 'We do not concede the motion of the column without the motion of the object that pushes it, so we do not concede that the motion of the hand occurs only after the motion of the stone; rather they are simultaneous in time, with the motion of the stone being consequent upon the motion of the hand, as its investigation passed.' (And among them is that he said: al-Jubba'i regarding the stone thrown by force upward when it returns descending: that its descending motion is generated from its ascending motion) based on his principle that motion is generated only from motion, not from reliance. And his son said: 'Rather it is generated from the descending reliance' which is in the stone, based on his principle that motion is generated only from reliance, not from motion. Therefore the author said: 'This is a branch of the preceding dispute.' Then he said: 'And on the two views there is arbitrary assignment and preference without a preferrer. As for the first: because if it is said: every motion of the successive motions...' His words: 'And from his hand's motion is generated the motion of what is upon it of hair and nails' because generation means that an act necessitates for its agent another act. Here there are not two motions, one the motion of the hand and the second the motion of the hair and nails; rather it is one motion attributed to the hand directly and to what is upon it by way of subordination, like the motion of a ship's rider. His words: 'As its investigation passed with his statement: and there is consideration.' Since what preceded was a prevention of the necessary overlapping and what al-Amidi mentioned was a prevention of posteriority, no repetition follows. His words: 'Then al-Jubba'i said, etc.' For if you say: Perhaps al-Jubba'i clings to the generation of the motion of the ship's rider on a smooth board from its motion, since there is no reliance of the ship upon the rider, so his motion is determined to be generated from its motion. I say: The intention is to refute what he mentioned in the example of the motion of the hand and the key. For al-Jubba'i's claim is that all generated motions are generated from motion, so refutation in one case is a refutation of his claim, and it is not a case of objection to an example rejected by the investigators. Yes, al-Jubba'i can make this one of the cases of motion's independence in generation, unless it is said: The generator here is also the reliance of the rider upon the ship. Since the commentator explicitly stated the reliance of the hand upon what is on it of hair and nails, the claim of the ship's reliance upon the rider in it is not far-fetched. His words: 'And from his hand's motion is generated the motion of what is upon it of hair and nails' because hair and nails have no life, so the rule of power does not extend to them so that they would be moved by power directly. In the constant ascent of the forcibly moved stone, an ascending motion is generated except the last motion, for it generates a descending motion. That is pure arbitrary assignment; rather the forcibly moved stone should go to infinity, because from each of its ascending motions another ascending motion would be generated—rather cessation. As for the second: because the descending reliance in the stone, if it necessitates descent, then let it necessitate it at first, i.e., at the beginning of motion. Also the statement that every ascending reliance necessitates, except the last of them, is a preference without a preferrer. Thus the acquired [motions] from the reliances. It was said in objection to the two views: But there is consideration, because the forced motion weakens whenever it gets far from the forcing principle due to the resistance of nature joined to the resistance of what is in the distance from the air which the moving object needs to penetrate. Thus its layers are not uniform so that they must be equal in rulings. Therefore the ascending motion may end in weakness to what necessitates the descending motion which is its opposite, not the ascending which is its like. For a thing affects its like only if it is extremely strong, and it may affect its opposite with its weakness. So the arbitrary assignment is repelled from al-Jubba'i. And the inherent reliance in the stone is overcome at first, i.e., at the beginning of motion, by the acquired [reliance] which the forcer supplied. Then the acquired weakens little by little by the resistance of nature and the penetrated air in its repulsion, until the acquired becomes overcome and the inherent prevails. Then the inherent reliance necessitates descent. And the answer regarding the generation of the reliances: what passed in the generation of motions. So the arbitrary assignment is repelled from his son as well. (And among them is that he said: Most Mu'tazila hold that there is no rest between the ascending and descending motion, because neither the inherent reliance necessitates it— for it necessitates descending motion—nor the acquired reliance, because it necessitates ascending motion, so rest is not generated. His words: 'If it is said: every motion of the successive motions...' And the ascending motion among the mutakallimun is two occurrences in two instants in two places or the first rest in the second place, and on this there is no doubt about the multiplicity of ascending motions in the supposed case, even if it is not multiple according to the philosophers—neither motion in the sense of intermediate nor in the sense of cutting, as known from their principles. His words: 'joined to the resistance of what is in the distance, etc.' This joining is frequent, not necessary. It would be necessary only if vacuum were impossible in the distance of the ascending motion, and there is no impossibility among the mutakallimun. And if the impossibility of vacuum is conceded, then what was mentioned would only be necessary if the air were still or moving contrary to the direction of motion towards it, but contrary to the acquired reliances. For if its motion were supposed to be upward, agreeing in its reliance with the acquired reliances, then there would be no resistance from what is in the distance either.

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered debate on the generation of motion, specifically whether motion generates motion independently or whether the underlying principle of reliance (i‘timād) is necessary. The text follows a sequence: first, a specific example of a hand and a key is given; second, a more general example of a stone thrown upward is introduced; third, the two positions (father al-Jubba'i and son Abu Hashim) are stated; fourth, an objection is raised against both; fifth, the objection is answered; and finally, additional commentary explains nuances.

The passage begins with the hand example: if a hand moves a key, is the key's motion generated from the hand's motion or from the hand's reliance? Al-Jubba'i argues that motion generates motion independently, citing the case of the hand moving and thereby moving hair and nails on it. He claims that attributing the key's motion to the hand's motion is more plausible than to its reliance. The response counters: the motion of hair and nails could be due to the hand's reliance and pushing, not the motion itself. Abu Hashim adds that the hand and the object it pushes (here referred to as 'the column' but earlier 'the stone') are simultaneous, so motion does not have independent generative power.

Then the stone example is given: a stone thrown upward by force eventually falls. Al-Jubba'i says the descending motion is generated from the ascending motion, consistent with his principle that motion generates motion. His son says it is generated from the descending reliance in the stone. The author (al-Iji) calls this a branch of the earlier dispute and states that both views involve arbitrary assignment (taḥakkum) and preference without a preferrer (tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ).

The commentary explains 'generation' (tawlīd) as one act necessitating another. Regarding the hand and hair/nails, the commentator argues it is one motion, not two, like a ship's rider. He also refutes a potential objection: al-Jubba'i might cite a ship's rider on a smooth board where motion seems independent of reliance; but the commentator says this also involves reliance (the rider's reliance on the ship). He concludes that hair and nails, being lifeless, are not directly moved by power but by the hand's reliance.

The refutation of the two views follows: against al-Jubba'i, the ascending motion weakens due to natural resistance and air, so its layers are not identical; thus the last ascending motion may generate its opposite (descent) without arbitrary assignment. Against the son, the inherent reliance is initially overcome by the acquired (mujtalab) reliance from the force, but as the acquired weakens, the inherent prevails and causes descent. Both arguments resolve the arbitrary assignment.

Finally, the text notes that most Mu'tazila hold there is no rest between ascending and descending motion, because neither inherent nor acquired reliance would generate rest. The commentary clarifies the concept of motion among mutakallimun (two occurrences in two instants) versus philosophers, and discusses the resistance of air and the possibility of vacuum, noting that the joining of resistances is not necessary if air moves agreeably.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Jubba'i and his son agree on the basic mechanism of motion generation, but they fundamentally differ on the role of i‘timād: al-Jubba'i holds that motion alone is sufficient, while his son insists that reliance is the true cause.
Correction: The text shows explicit contrasting principles: al-Jubba'i says motion generates from motion, his son says reliance generates motion. They are in direct opposition.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of contention between al-Jubba'i (father) and Abu Hashim (son) regarding the generation of motion?
A) Whether motion generates motion or reliance generates motion
B) Whether the stone moves upward by force or by nature
C) Whether the hand moves the key directly or through an intermediate
D) Whether hair and nails are alive or dead

Key Takeaway
The debate on motion generation reveals a fundamental split in Mu'tazili causality: al-Jubba'i prioritizes motion itself, while his son insists on the underlying principle of reliance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an action, do you feel it is your own effort or Allah's creation of the outcome? Does this lesson's debate challenge how you think about your daily reliance on Allah?
?
The son argued that reliance (i‘timād) is the true cause. In your life, what does it mean for you to rely on Allah while still moving? Is your trust in Allah active or passive?
?
Al-Jurjani warns against arbitrary preference without a preferrer. Are there areas in your faith where you accept beliefs without examining their foundation? What would it take to ground them in evidence?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
Forced Motion and Generation of Opposite Motion
Objections and answers on forced motion generation dynamics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from weakening of forced motion refutes the charge of arbitrariness in the generation of opposite motion.

Core Idea
Forced motion weakens with distance, making opposite motion possible.

This passage tackles a key objection against two Mu'tazilite views on forced motion. The objector claims that if forced motion generates only upward motions until the last, which generates downward, that is arbitrary. The answer lies in the physical reality that forced motion weakens due to resistance, so when it becomes weak enough, it generates the opposite motion rather than a like one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views under attack
Read the passage and spot the two views: the son's view (upward motions except last generates downward) and al-Jubbā'ī's view (each upward inclination necessitates motion except last).
The passage begins with 'the third observatory' and then presents the objection.
2
Understand the objection of arbitrariness
The objector says: why should the last motion generate downward instead of upward? That seems arbitrary. Also, if downward inclination necessitates descent, it should do so from the start.
3
Follow the answer: forced motion weakens
The answer explains that forced motion is not uniform; it weakens as it moves away from the forcing cause due to resistance. Thus it may become weak enough to generate the opposite motion.
The key phrase: 'its layers are not uniform'.
4
Note the principle: weak things affect opposites
A weak thing affects its opposite more easily than its like. So a weak upward motion generates a downward motion.
This repels the charge of arbitrariness from al-Jubbā'ī.

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل
في الصعود الثابتة للحجر المقسور ) ولدت حركة صاعدة إلا الحركة ( الأخيرة فإنها تولد ) حركة ( هابطة فهو تحكم ) بحت بل كان يجب أن يذهب ) الحجر المقسور ) إلى غير النهاية ) بأن يتولد من كل حركة من حركاته الصاعدة حركة أخرى صاعدة بل انقطاع ( وأما الثاني فلان (الاعتماد الهابط الذي في الحجر (إذا كان يوجب النزول فليوجبه (أولاً) أي في ابتداء الحركة، وأيضاً القول بأن كلاً اعتماداً صاعداً يوجب دون الاعتماد الأخير منها ترجيح بلا مرجح ( هكذا المجتلبة من الاعتمادات قيل في الاعتراض على الرأيين ) وفيه نظر لأن الحركة القسريه ( تضعف كلما بعدت عن المبدأ ) القاسر بسبب مقاومة الطبيعة لها منضمة إلى مقاومة ما في المسافة من الهواء الذي يحتاج المتحرك إلى خرقه ( فليست طبقاتها متماثلة) حتى يجب تساويها في الأحكام ( فقد تنتهي ( الحركة الصاعدة في الضعف (إلى ما يوجب ) أي إلى طبقة توجب الحركة ( النازلة ) التي هي ضدها دون الصاعدة التي هي مثلها، فإنَّ الشيء لا يؤثر في مثله إلا إذا كان قويا في الغاية وقد يؤثر في ضده مع ضعفه فاندفع التحكم عن الجبائي ( والاعتماد اللازم الذي في الحجر ( مغلوب في الأول ) أي في ابتداء الحركة ( بالمجتلب ) الذي أفاده القاسر ( ثم يضعف المجتلب قليلا قليلا بمقاومة الطبيعة والمخروق في دفعه ( حتى يصير) المجتلب ( مغلوباً) واللازم غالباً ( وحينئذ يوجب ) الاعتماد اللازم ( النزول ) . والجواب عن توليد الاعتمادات : ما مر في توليد الحركات، فاندفع التحكم عن ابنه أيضاً ، ( ومنها أنه قال أكثر المعتزلة: ليس بين الحركة الصاعدة والهابطة سكون إذ لا يوجبه الاعتماد لا اللازم فإنه يوجب الحركة الهابطة ) ولا المجتلب ) لأنه يقتضي الحركة الصاعدة فلا يتولد السكون منهما ولا شيء هناك غيرهما حتى يستند إليه السكون فلا سكون أصلاً. 

قوله : (إذا قيل : كل حركة من الحركات المتعاقبة والصعود الحركة عند المتكلمين كونان في آنين في مكانين أو السكون الأول في المكان الثاني وعلى هذا لا شبهة في تعدد الحركات المتصاعدة في الصورة المفروضة وإن لم يتعدد عند الحكماء لا الحركة بمعنى التوسط ولا بمعنى القطع كما علم من قواعدهم.
قوله : (منضمة إلى مقاومة ما في المسافة إلخ) هذا الانضمام أكثري وليس بلازم وإنما يلزم لو امتنع الخلاء في مسافة الحركة الصاعدة ولا امتناع عند المتكلمين ولو سلم امتناع الخلاء فإنما يلزم ما ذكر لو كان الهواء راكداً أو متحركاً إلى خلاف جهة الحركة إليها لكن مخالفاً للاعتمادات المجتلبة إذ لو قدر حركته إلى جهة العلو موافقاً في اعتماده للاعتمادات المجتلبة لم يوجد مقاومة ما في المسافة أيضاً.
The third observatory - the third objective: On inclination (al-i'timad), which is inclination (al-mayl). Regarding the established upward (motion) of a forcibly moved stone: it generates an upward motion except the last motion, for it generates a downward motion; this is sheer arbitrariness. Rather, the forcibly moved stone should go to infinity, in that from each of its upward motions another upward motion is generated without interruption. As for the second (view): the downward inclination that is in the stone, if it necessitates descent, it should necessitate it at first, i.e., at the beginning of motion. Also, the statement that each upward inclination necessitates (a motion) without the last of them is a preference without a preponderator. Thus the acquired inclinations. It was said in objection to the two views: But there is a consideration, because forced motion weakens every time it moves away from the forcing principle, due to the resistance of nature added to the resistance of the air in the distance that the moving body needs to penetrate. So its layers are not uniform such that they must be equal in effects. The upward motion may end in weakness to (a degree) that necessitates the downward motion which is its opposite, not the upward motion which is its like. For a thing does not affect its like except when it is extremely strong, but it may affect its opposite when it is weak. Thus the arbitrariness is repelled from al-Jubbā'ī. The necessary inclination in the stone is overcome at first, i.e., at the beginning of motion, by the acquired (inclination) that the forcer conferred. Then the acquired weakens little by little due to the resistance of nature and the pierced (air) in pushing it, until the acquired becomes overcome and the necessary becomes dominant. At that time the necessary inclination necessitates descent. The answer regarding the generation of inclinations is what was mentioned earlier regarding the generation of motions. Thus the arbitrariness is also repelled from his son. And among (the objections) is that he said: Most Mu'tazila hold that there is no rest between upward and downward motion, because neither inclination necessitates it: the necessary inclination necessitates downward motion, and the acquired inclination necessitates upward motion, so rest is not generated from them, and there is nothing else there to which rest could be attributed, so there is no rest at all. (Note:) His statement: 'If it is said: every motion among successive motions and upward motion according to the mutakallimun is two existences in two instants in two places, or the first rest in the second place, and on this there is no doubt about the multiplicity of upward motions in the supposed case, even if it is not multiple for the philosophers, neither motion in the sense of intermediate nor in the sense of cutting, as known from their principles.' (Note:) His statement: 'added to the resistance of what is in the distance, etc.' This addition is for the most part and not necessary; it would only be necessary if void were impossible in the distance of upward motion, and there is no impossibility according to the mutakallimun. And even if the impossibility of void is granted, what is mentioned would only follow if the air was stationary or moving contrary to the direction of motion towards it, but contrary to the acquired inclinations. For if its motion is supposed to be upward in agreement with its inclination to the acquired inclinations, there would be no resistance from what is in the distance either.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers regarding forced motion and the generation of motions and rest, followed by two explanatory notes. The passage first summarizes two views: the first view (likely of Abu Hashim, the son) holds that a forcibly moved stone generates upward motions except the last, which generates a downward motion, and this is criticized as arbitrary because it should generate successive upward motions indefinitely. The second view (of al-Jubbā'ī) holds that if the downward inclination necessitates descent, it should do so from the start, and that each upward inclination necessitates a motion except the last, which is an unjustified preference. The objector argues against both views: forced motion weakens with distance from the source due to resistance, so its intensities vary; thus when it becomes weak enough, it generates the opposite motion (downward) instead of a like motion (upward), since a weak thing can affect its opposite but not its like. This repels the charge of arbitrariness from al-Jubbā'ī. Then the answer regarding the generation of inclinations: the necessary (inherent) inclination is initially overcome by the acquired inclination, but as the acquired weakens due to resistance, the necessary becomes dominant and causes descent. This is the answer to the objection against the son as well. Then a second objection is raised: most Mu'tazila deny rest between upward and downward motion, because neither the necessary nor the acquired inclination produces rest; the former produces downward motion, the latter upward, and nothing else exists to cause rest. The first note clarifies the definition of motion among mutakallimun as two existences in two instants and two places, ensuring multiplicity of motions. The second note explains that the addition of air resistance is not necessary because void is possible for the mutakallimun, and even if void is impossible, resistance depends on air motion; if the air moves upward in agreement with the acquired inclinations, no resistance occurs.

Key Terms
حركة قسرية (forced motion)
اعتماد (inclination)
تولد (generation)
مجتلب (acquired)
لازم (necessary/inherent)
الجبائي (al-Jubbā'ī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forced motions are uniform and always generate like motions.
Correction: Forced motion weakens due to resistance; when weak, it generates opposite motion.

Checkpoint
Why does the forced motion eventually generate a downward motion according to the answer?
A) Because the acquired inclination becomes stronger.
B) Because the motion weakens and the necessary inclination overcomes.
C) Because the air resistance stops completely.
D) Because the motion reaches its natural place.

Key Takeaway
Forced motion's non-uniformity due to resistance explains why it generates opposite motion at the end, not arbitrarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, what is a good habit or spiritual practice that you started with strong external motivation (like a class, a friend's encouragement, or a new resolve) but later felt it weaken as you faced inner resistance or distractions? The lesson shows that forced motion weakens over time. What specific 'resistance' (e.g., laziness, bad company, worldly concerns) has been draining your acquired inclination? Write one concrete step you will take this week to reinforce it so it doesn't generate the opposite.
?
The passage states: 'A thing does not affect its like except when extremely strong, but it may affect its opposite when weak.' Think of a time when you were spiritually 'weak' and found yourself gravitating toward a sin or negligence—the opposite of what you intended. What does that reveal about the need to guard your spiritual strength? What are you actively doing to keep your faith 'extremely strong' so that it affects what is good rather than weakening you toward evil?
?
The stone has an inherent downward inclination that is temporarily overcome but eventually reasserts itself. Is there an 'inherent downward inclination' in your heart—a persistent bad habit, attachment, or character flaw—that you have managed to suppress but not truly transform? How can you move from merely suppressing it with external force (like guilt or peer pressure) to genuinely changing your nature so that the good becomes your own 'inherent inclination'? What would that require of you starting today?

Rest Between Motions and Definitional Clarifications
Objection on rest between motions and two clarificatory notes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position on the absence of rest between upward and downward motion and the definition of motion that supports it.

Core Idea
No rest exists between upward and downward motion because no inclination causes it.

The second objection concerns the existence of rest between upward and downward motion. Most Mu'tazila deny that rest can occur because neither the necessary nor the acquired inclination generates it. The passage then provides two explanatory notes clarifying the definition of motion among mutakallimun and the conditions for air resistance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about rest
The objector reports that most Mu'tazila deny rest between upward and downward motion. Why? Because neither the necessary inclination (which causes downward motion) nor the acquired inclination (which causes upward motion) can produce rest, and there is no third factor.
This is a separate objection from the first lesson.
2
Understand the reasoning
Rest would require a cause, but both inclinations present in the stone are for motion, not rest. So no rest can occur.
3
Examine the first explanatory note
The note clarifies that for mutakallimun, motion consists of two 'existences' in two instants in two places. This ensures that successive upward motions are distinct, even if philosophers deny multiplicity.
This supports the earlier discussion about multiple motions.
4
Examine the second explanatory note
This note discusses air resistance. It states that the addition of air resistance is not necessary because the mutakallimun allow void. Even if void is impossible, if the air moves in the same direction as the forced motion, there is no resistance.
This qualifies the earlier claim about resistance.

The third observatory - the third objective: On inclination (al-i'timad), which is inclination (al-mayl). Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers regarding forced motion and the generation of motions and rest, followed by two explanatory notes. The passage first summarizes two views: the first view (likely of Abu Hashim, the son) holds that a forcibly moved stone generates upward motions except the last, which generates a downward motion, and this is criticized as arbitrary because it should generate successive upward motions indefinitely. Then the answer regarding the generation of inclinations: the necessary (inherent) inclination is initially overcome by the acquired inclination, but as the acquired weakens due to resistance, the necessary becomes dominant and causes descent. This is the answer to the objection against the son as well. Then a second objection is raised: most Mu'tazila deny rest between upward and downward motion, because neither the necessary nor the acquired inclination produces rest; the former produces downward motion, the latter upward, and nothing else exists to cause rest. Al-Jubba'i's Argument for Rest Between Motions
Al-Jubba'i's argument for a rest between motions and the critique based on his denial of generation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's argument for a state of rest between upward and downward motions and why the argument contradicts his own principle on the generation of motion.

Core Idea
A seemingly logical argument can violate one's own foundational principles.

Al-Jubba'i, a prominent Mu'tazilite theologian, proposed that a moving body must have a moment of rest between ascending and descending. His reasoning relies on equilibrium of inclinations. However, as the commentator points out, this reasoning is inconsistent with al-Jubba'i's own doctrine that motion cannot be generated from inclination. In this lesson, we examine this argument and its internal flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Jubba'i's statement
Read the Arabic passage where al-Jubba'i states his view and provides his reasoning using equilibrium.
2
Identify the key concepts
Note the terms: upward inclination, downward inclination, equilibrium, rest. Understand how he argues for rest.
Equilibrium means neither inclination dominates, so no motion.
3
Understand the critique
Read the commentator's objection: al-Jubba'i's argument assumes motions are generated from inclinations, which he denies.
This is the core inconsistency.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the issue matters: it shows how a theologian's argument can violate their own principles.
This is a common pitfall in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الجبائي : لا أستبعد أن يكون بين الصاعدة والهابطة سكون وربما نصر مذهبه بأنَّ الاعتماد الصاعد غالب في أول الحال فيصعد الجسم إلى فوق ثم يغلب الاعتماد النازل فينزل الجسم إلى تحت ولا بد بينهما من التعادل فإن المغلوب حتى يصل إلى حد التعادل والتساوي وعنده أي عند التعادل يكون السكون إذ لا يتصور حينئذ حركة صاعدة ولا هابطة لأن الاعتمادين على حد التساوي، فلا غلبة لأحدهما على صاحبه وهو أي الاستدلال الذي نصر به مذهبه لا يوافق مذهبه لأنَّ هذا الاستدلال مبني على أنَّ الحركتين الصاعدة والهابطة متولدتان من الاعتمادين المجتلب واللازم، وأن السكون بين الحركتين متولد من مجموع الاعتمادين بسبب تساويهما. وقد مر أن الجبائي لا يجوز تولد الحركة والسكون من الاعتماد وهذا معنى قوله : إذ بحث توليد الاعتماد لهما أي للحركة والسكون خلاف أصله فلا يمكن له الاستدلال به بل حقه أن يقول : موافقاً لأصله الحركة الأخيرة من الحركات الثابتة للحجر المقسور مثلا توجب له سكوناً أولاً ثم حركة نازلة . فإن المتولد قد يتأخر عن المولد بالزمان عندهم كالقتل المتولد عن الرمي فلا محذور في تأخير الحركة النازلة بتوسط السكون عن الحركة الصاعدة المولدة إياها . وبالجملة فالمسألة فرع الاختلاف المتقدم فمن جوز أن تكون الحركة الصاعدة مولدة للهابطة لم يستبعد توليدها للسكون أيضاً فإنَّ

قوله : ( ولا شيء هناك غيرهما إلخ) مما يمكن إسناد السكون إليه فلا يرد أنه أن يجوز يكون لطبيعة الجسم إذ الطبيعة من حيث هي لا تقتضي شيئاً من الحركة والسكون ولا أنه يجوز أن يكون أثراً للواجب تعالى لأنهم لا يجوزون إسناد آثار الممكنات إليه تعالى ثم إنه مبني أنَّ السكون وجودي وإلا فيجوز أن يكون علته عدم علة الحركة على أنه عدم ملكة فلا بد له من علة وجودية .

قوله : (غالب) هذا يقتضي وجود الاعتماد الهابط وقد سبق أن مذهب الجبائي التضاد الاعتمادات مطلقاً، وبهذا الوجه أيضاً ينصر خلاف مذهبه.

قوله : (لا) توافق مذهبه لك أن تقول : لأجل عدم الموافقة قيل : ربما نصر، ولم يقل: واستدل عليه .

قوله : ( ولا شيء هناك غيرهما فإن قلت: لم لا يسندون السكون إلى إرادة المختار؟ قلت : مذهبهم النظر إلى الأسباب الظاهرة وتعليل بعض الممكنات ببعض منها والكلام هاهنا . عليه لأن مدعاهم نفي وجوب السكون ولا وجه لوجوب السكون هاهنا إلا من الأسباب الظاهرة .
Al-Jubbā'ī said: I do not consider it improbable that there is a rest (sukūn) between the upward and downward (motions). And perhaps one might support his school by (arguing) that the upward inclination is dominant at first, so the body ascends upwards; then the downward inclination dominates, so the body descends downwards; and between them there must be equilibrium (ta'ādul). For the overcome (motion) continues until it reaches the point of equilibrium and equality; and at that point, i.e., at equilibrium, there is rest, since at that time neither upward nor downward motion can be conceived, because the two inclinations are at the point of equality, so neither has dominance over the other. But this argument with which his school is supported does not agree with his own school, because this argument is based on the fact that the upward and downward motions are generated from the acquired and necessary inclinations, and that the rest between the two motions is generated from the combination of the two inclinations due to their equality. And it has already passed that al-Jubbā'ī does not permit the generation of motion and rest from inclination. This is the meaning of his statement: 'When the discussion of the generation of inclination for them, i.e., for motion and rest, is contrary to his principle, he cannot use this argument.' Rather, he ought to say, in agreement with his principle: 'The last motion among the established motions of the forcibly moved stone, for example, necessitates a rest for it first, then a downward motion.' For what is generated may be delayed in time from the generator according to them, like the murder generated from the shooting. So there is no objection to the delay of the downward motion through the mediation of rest from the upward motion that generated it. In summary, the issue is a branch of the previous disagreement. For whoever allows that the upward motion generates the downward motion does not consider it improbable that it also generates rest, for... 

Note: His statement: 'And nothing else there besides them, etc.' means from what could be attributed to rest. So it is not refuted that it could be attributed to the nature of the body, because nature as such does not necessitate anything of motion or rest; nor that it could be an effect of the Necessary Exalted, because they do not allow attributing effects of possibles to Him. Moreover, it is based on the fact that rest is existential; otherwise, its cause could be the absence of the cause of motion, as a privation of possession, but it must have an existential cause.

Note: His statement: 'dominant' entails the existence of the downward inclination, and it has already been passed that the school of al-Jubbā'ī is the opposition of inclinations absolutely. And in this way also, he supports what contradicts his school.

Note: His statement: 'does not agree with his school' – you may say: because of the lack of agreement, it was said 'perhaps he supported', and it was not said: 'and he argued for it'.

Note: His statement: 'and nothing else there besides them' – if you say: why do they not attribute rest to the will of the Chosen One? I say: Their school is to consider apparent causes and to explain some possibles by others, and the discussion here is about that. Because their claim is to negate the necessity of rest, and there is no basis for the necessity of rest here except from apparent causes.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubbā'ī's view on the existence of rest between upward and downward motions, followed by a critique showing its inconsistency with his own principles, and then several explanatory notes. Al-Jubbā'ī argues that there must be a state of equilibrium (ta'ādul) between the two opposing inclinations, during which no motion occurs, i.e., rest. The commentator points out that this argument assumes that motions are generated from inclinations (tawallud), but al-Jubbā'ī himself denies such generation. Therefore, the argument does not accord with his own school. The correct position for him would be that the last upward motion directly generates a rest, and then a downward motion, with a time delay, similar to the generation of murder from shooting. The issue is thus a branch of the earlier disagreement about whether motions generate other motions. The notes clarify: (1) Rest cannot be attributed to nature or to God; it must have an existential cause. (2) The word 'dominant' implies the existence of a downward inclination, which contradicts al-Jubbā'ī's doctrine that inclinations are mutually exclusive. (3) The phrase 'does not agree with his school' explains that the support is only tentative. (4) The final note addresses the question of why rest is not attributed to the will of God; the answer is that the mutakallimun focus on apparent causes and do not invoke divine will for such physical phenomena.

Key Terms
سكون (rest)
تعادل (equilibrium)
تولد الحركة (generation of motion)
الجبائي (al-Jubbā'ī)
أصل (principle)
الاعتماد الصاعد (upward inclination)
الاعتماد الهابط (downward inclination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Jubba'i's argument is logically sound and consistent with his school.
Correction: The argument assumes generation of motion from inclination, which al-Jubba'i denies, making it inconsistent.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say that al-Jubba'i's argument for rest does not agree with his own school?
A) Because al-Jubba'i denies the existence of rest.
B) Because the argument assumes generation of motion from inclination, which al-Jubba'i rejects.
C) Because the argument assumes two inclinations exist simultaneously.
D) Because al-Jubba'i believes motion is eternal.

Key Takeaway
A theologian's argument can be self-defeating if it relies on a principle they themselves reject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jubba'i built an argument on a premise he himself rejected. Are you aware of any belief you hold that is actually contradicted by another belief or action you take? What would it cost you to align them?
?
The passage uses the analogy of murder from shooting to explain delayed causation. How does this idea—that effects can be temporally distant from their causes—challenge your sense of responsibility for actions whose full consequences may only appear later?
?
If consistency is a hallmark of intellectual integrity, what step will you take today to identify and remove a contradiction in your own worldview, no matter how small?

The Causes of Rest: Nature, God, or Apparent Causes?
Explanatory notes on the cause of rest between motions and the principle of apparent causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rest must have an existential cause according to the mutakallimun, and why they do not attribute it to nature or God's direct will.

Core Idea
Physical phenomena are explained by apparent causes, not by divine will or nature alone.

After critiquing al-Jubba'i's argument, the commentator adds several clarifying notes. These address the question: what causes the rest between motions? The mutakallimun reject attributing it to nature or to God, insisting on an existential cause from among the apparent causes. This lesson unpacks those notes.

Guided Path
1
Read the notes
Read the Arabic notes that follow the main discussion about the cause of rest.
These are marked by قوله in the text.
2
Identify the rejected causes
The notes reject nature and the Necessary as causes for rest. Understand why.
Nature as such does not necessitate anything; God's effects are only through apparent causes.
3
Understand the existential cause requirement
Rest is existential, so it must have an existential cause, not merely the absence of motion.
This is a key principle in kalam: attributes require causes.
4
Reflect on the principle of apparent causes
The final note explains the mutakallimun's method: they refer physical phenomena to apparent causes, not directly to God's will.
This is a methodological choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الجبائي : لا أستبعد أن يكون بين الصاعدة والهابطة سكون وربما نصر مذهبه بأنَّ الاعتماد الصاعد غالب في أول الحال فيصعد الجسم إلى فوق ثم يغلب الاعتماد النازل فينزل الجسم إلى تحت ولا بد بينهما من التعادل فإن المغلوب حتى يصل إلى حد التعادل والتساوي وعنده أي عند التعادل يكون السكون إذ لا يتصور حينئذ حركة صاعدة ولا هابطة لأن الاعتمادين على حد التساوي، فلا غلبة لأحدهما على صاحبه وهو أي الاستدلال الذي نصر به مذهبه لا يوافق مذهبه لأنَّ هذا الاستدلال مبني على أنَّ الحركتين الصاعدة والهابطة متولدتان من الاعتمادين المجتلب واللازم، وأن السكون بين الحركتين متولد من مجموع الاعتمادين بسبب تساويهما. وقد مر أن الجبائي لا يجوز تولد الحركة والسكون من الاعتماد وهذا معنى قوله : إذ بحث توليد الاعتماد لهما أي للحركة والسكون خلاف أصله فلا يمكن له الاستدلال به بل حقه أن يقول : موافقاً لأصله الحركة الأخيرة من الحركات الثابتة للحجر المقسور مثلا توجب له سكوناً أولاً ثم حركة نازلة . فإن المتولد قد يتأخر عن المولد بالزمان عندهم كالقتل المتولد عن الرمي فلا محذور في تأخير الحركة النازلة بتوسط السكون عن الحركة الصاعدة المولدة إياها . وبالجملة فالمسألة فرع الاختلاف المتقدم فمن جوز أن تكون الحركة الصاعدة مولدة للهابطة لم يستبعد توليدها للسكون أيضاً فإنَّ

قوله : ( ولا شيء هناك غيرهما إلخ) مما يمكن إسناد السكون إليه فلا يرد أنه أن يجوز يكون لطبيعة الجسم إذ الطبيعة من حيث هي لا تقتضي شيئاً من الحركة والسكون ولا أنه يجوز أن يكون أثراً للواجب تعالى لأنهم لا يجوزون إسناد آثار الممكنات إليه تعالى ثم إنه مبني أنَّ السكون وجودي وإلا فيجوز أن يكون علته عدم علة الحركة على أنه عدم ملكة فلا بد له من علة وجودية .

قوله : (غالب) هذا يقتضي وجود الاعتماد الهابط وقد سبق أن مذهب الجبائي التضاد الاعتمادات مطلقاً، وبهذا الوجه أيضاً ينصر خلاف مذهبه.

قوله : (لا) توافق مذهبه لك أن تقول : لأجل عدم الموافقة قيل : ربما نصر، ولم يقل: واستدل عليه .

قوله : ( ولا شيء هناك غيرهما فإن قلت: لم لا يسندون السكون إلى إرادة المختار؟ قلت : مذهبهم النظر إلى الأسباب الظاهرة وتعليل بعض الممكنات ببعض منها والكلام هاهنا . عليه لأن مدعاهم نفي وجوب السكون ولا وجه لوجوب السكون هاهنا إلا من الأسباب الظاهرة .
Al-Jubbā'ī said: I do not consider it improbable that there is a rest (sukūn) between the upward and downward (motions). And perhaps one might support his school by (arguing) that the upward inclination is dominant at first, so the body ascends upwards; then the downward inclination dominates, so the body descends downwards; and between them there must be equilibrium (ta'ādul). For the overcome (motion) continues until it reaches the point of equilibrium and equality; and at that point, i.e., at equilibrium, there is rest, since at that time neither upward nor downward motion can be conceived, because the two inclinations are at the point of equality, so neither has dominance over the other. But this argument with which his school is supported does not agree with his own school, because this argument is based on the fact that the upward and downward motions are generated from the acquired and necessary inclinations, and that the rest between the two motions is generated from the combination of the two inclinations due to their equality. And it has already passed that al-Jubbā'ī does not permit the generation of motion and rest from inclination. This is the meaning of his statement: 'When the discussion of the generation of inclination for them, i.e., for motion and rest, is contrary to his principle, he cannot use this argument.' Rather, he ought to say, in agreement with his principle: 'The last motion among the established motions of the forcibly moved stone, for example, necessitates a rest for it first, then a downward motion.' For what is generated may be delayed in time from the generator according to them, like the murder generated from the shooting. So there is no objection to the delay of the downward motion through the mediation of rest from the upward motion that generated it. In summary, the issue is a branch of the previous disagreement. For whoever allows that the upward motion generates the downward motion does not consider it improbable that it also generates rest, for... 

Note: His statement: 'And nothing else there besides them, etc.' means from what could be attributed to rest. So it is not refuted that it could be attributed to the nature of the body, because nature as such does not necessitate anything of motion or rest; nor that it could be an effect of the Necessary Exalted, because they do not allow attributing effects of possibles to Him. Moreover, it is based on the fact that rest is existential; otherwise, its cause could be the absence of the cause of motion, as a privation of possession, but it must have an existential cause.

Note: His statement: 'dominant' entails the existence of the downward inclination, and it has already been passed that the school of al-Jubbā'ī is the opposition of inclinations absolutely. And in this way also, he supports what contradicts his school.

Note: His statement: 'does not agree with his school' – you may say: because of the lack of agreement, it was said 'perhaps he supported', and it was not said: 'and he argued for it'.

Note: His statement: 'and nothing else there besides them' – if you say: why do they not attribute rest to the will of the Chosen One? I say: Their school is to consider apparent causes and to explain some possibles by others, and the discussion here is about that. Because their claim is to negate the necessity of rest, and there is no basis for the necessity of rest here except from apparent causes.

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubbā'ī's view on the existence of rest between upward and downward motions, followed by a critique showing its inconsistency with his own principles, and then several explanatory notes. Al-Jubbā'ī argues that there must be a state of equilibrium (ta'ādul) between the two opposing inclinations, during which no motion occurs, i.e., rest. The commentator points out that this argument assumes that motions are generated from inclinations (tawallud), but al-Jubbā'ī himself denies such generation. Therefore, the argument does not accord with his own school. The correct position for him would be that the last upward motion directly generates a rest, and then a downward motion, with a time delay, similar to the generation of murder from shooting. The issue is thus a branch of the earlier disagreement about whether motions generate other motions. The notes clarify: (1) Rest cannot be attributed to nature or to God; it must have an existential cause. (2) The word 'dominant' implies the existence of a downward inclination, which contradicts al-Jubbā'ī's doctrine that inclinations are mutually exclusive. (3) The phrase 'does not agree with his school' explains that the support is only tentative. (4) The final note addresses the question of why rest is not attributed to the will of God; the answer is that the mutakallimun focus on apparent causes and do not invoke divine will for such physical phenomena.

علة (cause)
وجودي (existential)
طبيعة (nature)
الواجب (the Necessary)
أسباب ظاهرة (apparent causes)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think rest is simply the absence of motion and needs no cause.
Correction: In kalam, rest is considered existential and therefore requires a cause, just as motion does.

Checkpoint
According to the notes, why can rest not be attributed to the nature of the body?
A) Because nature is not a cause of anything.
B) Because nature as such does not necessitate anything of motion or rest.
C) Because nature is eternal.
D) Because nature is an apparent cause.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, rest is an existential quality that must be explained by apparent causes, not by nature or divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a period of peace or rest in your life, do you simply thank God without reflecting on the means He provided to bring that rest? How can you more actively acknowledge both the divine source and the apparent causes He used?
?
The mutakallimun refused to attribute rest to nature or directly to God. In your own struggles, do you sometimes blame 'nature' (e.g., 'that's just how I am') or expect God to change things without using means? Which apparent causes might God be calling you to address?
?
The passage says rest is not just absence of motion but a positive state requiring a cause. Think of a time you were spiritually 'at rest'. Was it merely due to a lack of trials, or was there a positive cause (e.g., trust in God, consistent worship)? Will you seek that cause intentionally?
