Al-Jubba'ī on Rest Between Motions and Its Critique
Presentation and critique of al-Jubba'ī's argument for rest between motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'ī's argument for the existence of rest between upward and downward motion, and the critique that it is inconsistent with his own causal principles.

Core Idea
Al-Jubba'ī's proof for rest uses a model of generation he himself rejects.

This lesson examines a subtle theological dispute about whether a body must rest between ascending and descending motion. Al-Jubba'ī argues for such rest based on a balance of opposing tendencies (i‘timād), but al-Jurjani shows that the argument relies on a theory of generation (tawallud) that al-Jubba'ī explicitly denies. We will trace the argument step by step and see the critique unfold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: premise, argument, critique, alternative.
2
Identify al-Jubba'ī's argument
Find the claim about rest and the reasoning about balance of tendencies. Note that he says 'I do not consider it improbable' – a modest claim.
The argument hinges on the idea that the overcome tendency must pass through a state of equality.
3
Understand the critique
The commentator points out that al-Jubba'ī's argument assumes that motion and rest are generated from tendencies, which contradicts al-Jubba'ī's own doctrine that generation from tendencies is not permitted.
This is the key inconsistency: using a premise one denies.
4
Examine the alternative account
The correct account consistent with al-Jubba'ī's principles is that the last motion (e.g., of a stone) necessitates rest first, then descending motion, with delay possible.
This avoids the need for balance and generation.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this debate illustrates the centrality of causal theory in kalām. The disagreement about generation (tawallud) affects physical explanations.
The issue is 'a branch of the preceding disagreement' – tying back to a foundational dispute.

المرصد الثالث - المقصد الثالث : في الاعتماد وهو الميل
لا يصير غالباً منهما ولا شيء هناك غيرهما حتى يستند إليه السكون فلا سكون أصلاً. (وقال الجبائي : لا استبعد) أن يكون بين الصاعدة والهابطة سكون ( وربما نصر مذهبه بأنَّ الاعتماد الصاعد غالب ( في أول الحال ( فيصعد ( الجسم إلى فوق (ثم يغلب) الاعتماد ( النازل فينزل ( الجسم إلى تحت ) ولا بد بينهما من التعادل ) فإن المغلوب حتى يصل إلى حد التعادل والتساوي ( وعنده ) أي عند التعادل ( يكون السكون إذ لا يتصور حينئذ حركة صاعدة ولا هابطة لأن الاعتمادين على حد ) التساوي، فلا غلبة لأحدهما على صاحبه ) وهو) أي الاستدلال الذي نصر به مذهبه (لا يوافق مذهبه). لأنَّ هذا الاستدلال مبني على أنَّ الحركتين الصاعدة والهابطة متولدتان من الاعتمادين المجتلب ،واللازم، وأن السكون بين الحركتين متولد من مجموع الاعتمادين بسبب تساويهما. وقد مر أن الجبائي لا يجوز تولد الحركة والسكون من الاعتماد وهذا معنى قوله : ( إذ بحث توليد الاعتماد لهما ) أي للحركة والسكون ( خلاف أصله ) فلا يمكن له الاستدلال به بل حقه أن يقول : ) موافقاً لأصله (الحركة الأخيرة) من الحركات الثابتة للحجر المقسور مثلا ( توجب ) له (سكوناً ) أولاً ( ثم حركة ) نازلة . ( فإن المتولد قد يتأخر عن المولد بالزمان عندهم) كالقتل المتولد عن الرمي فلا محذور في تأخير الحركة النازلة بتوسط السكون عن الحركة الصاعدة المولدة إياها . ( وبالجملة فالمسألة فرع الاختلاف المتقدم) فمن جوز أن تكون الحركة الصاعدة مولدة للهابطة لم يستبعد توليدها للسكون أيضاً فإنَّ

قوله : ( ولا شيء هناك غيرهما إلخ) أي مما يمكن إسناد السكون إليه فلا يرد أنه أن يجوز يكون لطبيعة الجسم إذ الطبيعة من حيث هي لا تقتضي شيئاً من الحركة والسكون ولا أنه يجوز أن يكون أثراً للواجب تعالى لأنهم لا يجوزون إسناد آثار الممكنات إليه تعالى ثم إنه مبني أنَّ السكون وجودي وإلا فيجوز أن يكون علته عدم علة الحركة على أنه عدم ملكة فلا بد له من علة وجودية .

قوله : (غالب) هذا يقتضي وجود الاعتماد الهابط وقد سبق أن مذهب الجبائي التضاد الاعتمادات مطلقاً، وبهذا الوجه أيضاً ينصر خلاف مذهبه.

قوله : (لا توافق مذهبه) لك أن تقول : لأجل عدم الموافقة قيل : ربما نصر، ولم يقل: واستدل عليه .

قوله : ( ولا شيء هناك غيرهما فإن قلت: لم لا يسندون السكون إلى إرادة المختار؟ قلت : مذهبهم النظر إلى الأسباب الظاهرة وتعليل بعض الممكنات ببعض منها والكلام هاهنا . عليه لأن مدعاهم نفي وجوب السكون ولا وجه لوجوب السكون هاهنا إلا من الأسباب الظاهرة .

قوله : (فمن جوز إلخ) الأظهر أن يقال : فمن قال : المولد للحركة والسكون هو الاعتماد لم يجوز السكون بين الصاعدة والهابطة إذ لا اعتماد فلا سكون ومن قال: المولد لهما الحركة جوز أن تكون الحركة الصاعدة مولدة للسكون إلا أن الشارح قدس سره راعى القرب.

قوله : ( أبعد من الثاني) أي لكونهما متضادين بخلاف الحركة الصاعدة مع السكون إذ لا تضاد إلا بين الأنواع الأخيرة من جنس واحد.

قوله : ( ومن لم يجوز ذلك إلخ) فيه أنَّ عدم التجويز يستلزم عدم الارتكاب لا ارتكاب العدم.

قوله : ( وأما قضية التعادل إلخ) أي لا نسلم أنه المغلوب لا يكون غالباً إلا بعد التعادل .

فلا يلزم سكون أصلاً .
Third Observatory - Third Objective: On al-I‘timād (اعتماد), which is inclination.
It does not become dominant from them, and there is nothing else there besides them to which rest could be attributed, so there is no rest at all. (Al-Jubba'ī said: I do not consider it improbable) that there is rest between the ascending and descending (motion). (And perhaps he supported his doctrine by [saying] that the ascending tendency (al-i‘timād al-ṣā‘id) is dominant (in the first state), so the body ascends upward; then the descending tendency becomes dominant, so the body descends downward; and between them there must be a state of balance (al-ta‘ādul); for that which is overcome [continues] until it reaches the limit of balance and equality; and at it (i.e., at the time of balance) rest occurs, because then no ascending or descending motion can be conceived, since the two tendencies are at the limit of equality, so neither overcomes the other. And it (i.e., the inference by which he supported his doctrine) does not agree with his doctrine. Because this inference is based on the fact that the two motions, ascending and descending, are generated (mutawallidatān) from the two tendencies—the drawn (mujtalab) and the necessary (lāzim)—and that the rest between the two motions is generated from the sum of the two tendencies due to their equality. And it has passed that al-Jubba'ī does not permit the generation (tawallud) of motion and rest from tendency; and this is the meaning of his statement: 'Since discussing the generation of tendency for them (i.e., for motion and rest) is contrary to his principle.' So he cannot use this inference; rather, he should say—in agreement with his principle—that the last motion (of the constant motions of a forcibly moved stone, for example) necessitates rest first, then a descending motion. For the generated (al-mutawallad) may be delayed in time from the generator (al-muwallid) according to them, like killing generated from throwing; so there is no harm in delaying the descending motion, through the intermediary of rest, from the ascending motion that generated it. (And in summary, the issue is a branch of the preceding disagreement.) So whoever permits that the ascending motion generates the descending one does not consider it improbable that it also generates rest, because...

His statement: 'And there is nothing else besides them, etc.'—i.e., nothing to which rest could be attributed. So it is not objected that it could be attributed to the nature of the body, since nature as such does not necessitate anything of motion or rest; nor that it could be an effect of the Necessary Exalted, because they do not permit attributing the effects of contingent things to Him. Moreover, it is built on the premise that rest is existent (wujūdī); otherwise, its cause could be the absence of the cause of motion, but it is a privation of disposition (‘adam malaka), so it must have an existential cause.

His statement: 'Dominant'—this necessitates the existence of the descending tendency, and it has preceded that al-Jubba'ī's doctrine is the absolute opposition of tendencies; in this respect also, it supports [a view] contrary to his doctrine.

His statement: 'Does not agree with his doctrine'—you may say: because of the disagreement, it was said: 'perhaps he supported', and he did not say: 'and he adduced evidence for it'.

His statement: 'And there is nothing else besides them; for if you say: Why do they not attribute rest to the will of the Free Agent? I say: Their doctrine is to consider apparent causes and to explain some contingent things by others; and the discourse here is about that, because their claim is the negation of the necessity of rest, and there is no ground for the necessity of rest here except from apparent causes.'

His statement: 'Whoever permits, etc.'—the more evident is to say: Whoever says that the generator of motion and rest is tendency does not permit rest between the ascending and descending because there is no tendency, so no rest; and whoever says that the generator of them is motion does permit that the ascending motion generates rest—except that the commentator, may his secret be sanctified, observed proximity.

His statement: 'More improbable than the second'—i.e., because they are opposite, unlike ascending motion with rest, since opposition is only between the ultimate species of a single genus.

His statement: 'And whoever does not permit that, etc.'—in it [the implication] is that non-permission necessitates non-commitment, not commitment to non-existence.

His statement: 'As for the issue of balance, etc.'—i.e., we do not concede that the overcome does not become dominant except after balance.

So no rest necessarily occurs at all.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques al-Jubba'ī's argument for the existence of rest (sukūn) between ascending and descending motion. It begins by stating the premise: there is nothing else besides the two tendencies to which rest could be attributed, so if those tendencies are not present, there is no rest. Then al-Jubba'ī's view is quoted: he does not find it improbable that rest occurs between the two motions. He supports this by suggesting that the ascending tendency may be dominant initially, causing the body to rise; then the descending tendency becomes dominant, causing descent; but between these two states there must be a moment of balance (ta‘ādul) where the tendencies are equal, resulting in rest. The commentator then points out that this reasoning is inconsistent with al-Jubba'ī's own doctrine, because it relies on the idea that motion and rest are generated (tawallud) from the tendencies—something al-Jubba'ī rejects. Instead, the correct account consistent with his principle would be that the last motion (e.g., of a forcibly moved stone) necessitates rest first, then a descending motion, since the generated can be delayed in time. The passage then explains several glosses: the first clarifies that there is no other cause to attribute rest to (nature is indifferent, and God is not directly invoked for contingent effects). The second notes that the mention of a dominant tendency implies the existence of the opposite tendency, contrary to al-Jubba'ī's view of absolute opposition. The third explains why the argument is said to 'perhaps' support, not 'prove'. The fourth answers an objection about attributing rest to God's will: the mutakallimūn look to apparent causes. The fifth distinguishes two positions: one (where tendency is the generator) cannot allow rest; the other (where motion is the generator) can. The sixth explains that opposition between upward and downward motion is stronger than between upward motion and rest. The seventh notes that non-permission implies non-commitment. The eighth rejects the claim that the overcome must first reach balance. The conclusion is that rest does not necessarily occur.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that al-Jubba'ī's argument is internally consistent with his own principles.
Correction: Al-Jubba'ī's argument uses the concept of generation (tawallud) of motion from tendency, but he himself does not permit such generation. Therefore the argument is not valid according to his own doctrine.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that al-Jubba'ī's reasoning does not match his own principles?
A) Because al-Jubba'ī denies the existence of tendencies.
B) Because al-Jubba'ī does not allow motion and rest to be generated from tendency.
C) Because al-Jubba'ī claims rest is impossible.
D) Because al-Jubba'ī believes motion is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'ī's argument for rest between motions fails because it relies on a causal theory (generation from tendency) that he himself rejects; the correct account consistent with his principles uses delayed generation from motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your own understanding of Allah's creation where you hold inconsistent premises, just as al-Jubba'ī did? What would it take for you to reconcile them?
?
Al-Jubba'ī's argument for rest seemed plausible but contradicted his own principles. In your life, when have you argued for something that felt right but later realized it conflicted with deeper commitments? How did you correct it?
?
This lesson shows how a small inconsistency in a causal theory can unravel an entire argument. What small inconsistency in your own character or belief might be undermining your spiritual consistency before Allah?

The Fourth Proof: Light Does Not Transmit Blackness
Ibn Sina's fourth proof and its defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's fourth proof that whiteness is not light, based on the phenomenon of color reflection.

Core Idea
Reflection shows that whiteness cannot be identical to light.

This lesson presents one of Ibn Sina's key arguments against identifying whiteness with light. The proof uses a simple experiment: light reflecting off a black surface should only transmit blackness if only black and white existed, but we see other colors, proving whiteness is distinct from light. We will walk through the proof, the objection from Imam al-Razi, and the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation to get an overview of the proof and its defense.
2
Understand the proof's logic
Trace the steps: (a) if only black and white existed, (b) reflection should only produce blackness, (c) but we see red, green, etc., therefore (d) whiteness is not light.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Examine the objection
Imam al-Razi suggests that the perceived colors might be mere appearances without real existence.
This challenges the reality of the observed colors.
4
See the response
The commentator argues that reflection requires actual qualities, so the objection fails.
The response defends the reality of the colors.
5
Connect to the bigger picture
This proof supports the view that whiteness is a real quality distinct from light.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتركب هذه الألوان المتوسطة عنها لاتحد الطريق إشارة إلى ما نقلناه عنه الرابع: الضوء لا ينقل السواد تجربة أي إذا انعكس الضوء من جسم صقيل أسود إلى جسم آخر لم يصر المنعكس إليه أسود فلو لم يكن إلا سواد وبياض على الوجه الذي ذكر وجب أن يصير المنعكس إليه أحمر وأخضر لأنَّ هذه الألوان حينئذ إنما هي لاختلاط الشفاف بالمظلم والانعكاس إنما يكون من الأجزاء الشفافة دون السود فوجب أن لا ينعكس إلا البياض الذي هو الضوء وهو باطل قطعاً. قال الإمام الرازي: وفي هذين الوجهين أيضاً نظر لجواز أن يوجد هناك أمور مختلفة لأجلها يحس بالكيفيات المختلفة وإن لم يكن لها وجود في الحقيقة كما جاز ذلك في اللون الواحد الخامس : أن الطبخ يفعل في الجص والنورة من البياض ما لا يفعله السحق والتصويل أي الدق فليس بياضهما بسبب أنَّ الطبخ أفادهما تخلخلاً وتفرق أجزاء فداخلهما الهواء
قوله : إنما هي لأجل اختلاط الشفاف أي الجسم الشفاف بالمظلم فإنه إذا كان الجسم شفافاً محضاً نفذ الهواء المستضيء فيه فيتخيل البياض وإذا كان مظلماً كان سواداً وإذا اختلطا تختلط الألوان المختلفة على حسب مراتب الاختلاط .
قوله : فوجب أن لا ينعكس إلخ إذ لا انعكاس إلا عن الموجود ولا موجود إلا السواد ولا انعكاس منه، أو الضوء الذي يخيل أنه بياض فاندفع ما قيل : إنه يجوز أن يكون للتركيب والانضمام مدخل في خصوص الانعكاس ولا يجب أن لا ينعكس إلا البياض. نعم يمكن منع الانعكاس حقيقة وإنما هو تخيلي وهذا ما ذكره الإمام. قوله : أن يوجد هناك أي في صورة الاتحاد بطريق آخر غير الاغبرار وصورة الانعكاس أمور مختلفة.
قوله : وإن لم يكن لها وجود إلخ بل الموجود إنما هو السواد أو الضوء الذي يخيل أنه بياض فيكون وجود تلك الكيفيات وانعكاسها متخيلاً. قوله : أنَّ الطبخ أفادهما تخلخلاً إلخ وما قيل: إنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون لتفاوت التخلخلين. فإنَّ الطبخ يكثر الحجم دون السحق فمناف لما قالوه في بياض الزجاج المسحوق .
قوله : الضوء لا ينقل السواد تجربة قال الإمام في الملخص: الأرجوانية والنيروزية والخضرة الناصعة والحمرة الصافية ألوان مشرقة قريبة من طباع الضوء ولذلك ينعكس إلى غيرها كالأضواء والغيرة والكهبة والعودية والسواد وأمثالها مظلمة ولذلك لا تنعكس إلى غيرها . قوله : وجب أن أن لا يصير المنعكس إليه أحمر وأخضر وإذا صار أحمر وأخضر وجب أن يكون هناك شيء مرئي غير السواد والبياض على الوجه الذي ذكر أعني على طريق التخيل، وليس غير الضوء كما عرفت فوجب أن يكون الضوء غيرهما فثبت بياض ليس أصله ضوءاً .
قوله: فوجب أن لا ينعكس إلا البياض قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون للتركيب والانضمام مدخل في خصوص الانعكاس ؟ فلا يجب أن لا ينعكس إلا البياض.
قوله : وإلا كان السحق والتصويل يفعلان فيهما مثل ما يفعل الطبخ بل بياضهما أن الطبخ أفادهما مزاجاً بسبب يوجب ذلك الابيضاض . قوله : وإلا كان السحق إلخ قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك لتفاوت التخلخلين والحق هذا فإنَّ الطبخ يكثر الحجم بخلاف السحق .
These intermediate colors are composed... from what we transmitted from him the fourth: light does not transmit blackness by experience, i.e., if light is reflected from a polished black body to another body, the body to which it is reflected does not become black. So if there were only blackness and whiteness in the manner mentioned, then the reflected [light] would have to become red and green, because these colors at that time are only due to the mixture of the transparent with the dark, and reflection is only from the transparent parts, not from the black, so it would have to reflect only whiteness, which is light, and that is absolutely false. Imam al-Razi said: In these two aspects there is also consideration because it is possible that different things exist there due to which different qualities are perceived even if they have no existence in reality, just as that is possible for a single color. Fifth: cooking does to plaster and lime that whiteness which grinding and pulverizing do not, i.e., pounding; so their whiteness is not because cooking gave them looseness and separation of parts so that air entered them. He said: They are only due to the mixture of the transparent, i.e., the transparent body, with the dark; for if the body is purely transparent, the illuminated air permeates it and whiteness is imagined, and if it is dark, it is black, and if they mix, the different colors mix according to the degrees of mixture. He said: Therefore reflection would have to be only from what exists, and nothing exists except blackness and no reflection from it, or the light that is imagined to be whiteness; so what was said is refuted: that composition and conjunction can have an entry in the particularity of reflection, and it is not necessary that only whiteness be reflected. Yes, it is possible to deny reflection in reality, and it is only imaginary, and that is what the Imam mentioned. He said: That there exist there, i.e., in the form of union by another way other than dustiness and the form of reflection, different things. He said: And even if they have no existence etc., rather what exists is only blackness or the light that is imagined to be whiteness, so the existence of those qualities and their reflection is imaginary. He said: That cooking gave them looseness etc., and it was said: Why should it not be permissible that it is due to the difference of the two loosenesses? For cooking increases volume without grinding, which contradicts what they said about the whiteness of powdered glass. He said: Light does not transmit blackness by experience; the Imam said in al-Mulakhkhas: Violet, niruzy, bright green, and pure red are bright colors close to the nature of light, therefore they are reflected to others; like lights, iron-gray, ash-gray, wood-color, and blackness and their likes are dark, therefore they are not reflected to others. He said: It would have to be that what is reflected to it does not become red or green, and if it becomes red or green, then there would have to be a visible thing other than blackness and whiteness in the manner mentioned, i.e., by way of imagination, and there is nothing other than light as you know, so light would have to be other than them, so whiteness is established whose origin is not light. He said: Therefore only whiteness would have to be reflected. It was said: Why is it not permissible for composition and conjunction to have an entry in the particularity of reflection? So it is not necessary that only whiteness be reflected. He said: Otherwise, grinding and pulverizing would do to them the same as cooking does; rather their whiteness is that cooking gave them a mixture due to which that whitening occurs. He said: Otherwise, grinding etc. It was said: Why is it not permissible that that is due to the difference of the two loosenesses? And the truth is this: cooking increases volume unlike grinding.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and defense of two proofs from Ibn Sina against the view that whiteness is identical with light. The first proof (the fourth in the series) argues from the phenomenon of reflection: if only black and white existed, then when light reflects off a black surface, it should only transmit blackness, but in fact it produces colors like red and green. This shows that whiteness is not simply light. Imam al-Razi raises an objection: the different colors might be mere appearances without real existence. The commentator then explains the argument further: reflection occurs only from transparent parts, not from black, so only whiteness (light) should reflect. But since we see colors, there must be something else. The second proof (the fifth) is about cooking and grinding: cooking plaster and lime makes them whiter than grinding, showing that the whiteness is due to a change in constitution (a mixture), not just freedom from air. An objection about differences in looseness is considered and answered. The passage thus establishes that whiteness has a real existence independent of light.

الضوء
الانعكاس
السواد
البياض
الشفاف
المظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whiteness is just an effect of light.
Correction: This proof shows that whiteness can be reflected independently of light, and causes other colors, so it is a real quality.

Checkpoint
What is the key observation in the fourth proof?
A) Light reflected from a black surface transmits blackness.
B) Light reflected from a black surface does not transmit blackness.
C) Light reflected from a white surface transmits colors.
D) Light reflected from any surface transmits only whiteness.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof demonstrates that whiteness is a real quality distinct from light, as reflection reveals colors beyond black and white.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that careful observation can overturn a seemingly obvious assumption. Is there an assumption you hold about your own spiritual state (e.g., 'I am a good person') that you have never questioned? What would it take for you to examine it with the same rigor Ibn Sina applied? Will you set aside time this week to reflect on one such assumption?
?
Imam al-Razi suggested the observed colors might be illusions, but the commentator insisted they are real. In your life, do you dismiss uncomfortable truths as 'illusions' to avoid change? When you see a flaw in your character, do you treat it as real and seek to transform it, or do you rationalize it away? Commit today to facing one uncomfortable truth about yourself.
?
The argument relies on the reality of qualities like whiteness and blackness. How seriously do you take the qualities of your own heart—like sincerity, arrogance, or gratitude? Do you treat them as real and actionable, or as vague feelings? What concrete step can you take tonight to cultivate one good quality or eliminate one bad one?

The Fifth Proof: Whiteness from Cooking and Grinding
Ibn Sina's fifth proof and its defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's fifth proof that whiteness is not light, based on the different effects of cooking and grinding on substances like plaster.

Core Idea
Cooking produces whiteness in a way grinding cannot, proving whiteness is a real quality.

The fifth proof takes a different approach, examining how whiteness is produced in materials like plaster and lime. Cooking these substances yields a whiteness that simple grinding cannot achieve, even though both processes create air spaces. This shows that whiteness is due to a specific mixture (mizaj) created by heat, not merely the presence of light. We will explore the proof, an objection about differences in looseness, and the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation to understand the fifth proof and its reasoning.
2
Compare cooking and grinding
Both create air pockets, but only cooking produces intense whiteness.
3
Identify the argument
If whiteness were just light entering air spaces, grinding should produce the same effect, but it doesn't — so whiteness must be a distinct quality.
4
Consider the objection
Some argue the difference is due to degree of looseness (takhalkhul), not a different cause.
5
Evaluate the response
The commentator argues that cooking creates a different mixture (mizaj) that causes whitening, not just more air.

Misconception & Correction

According to the fifth proof, why does cooking produce more whiteness in plaster than grinding?
A) Because cooking creates more air pockets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever reduced something complex in your life to a single, simplistic cause, ignoring the deeper qualitative changes needed? How might you reconsider your own 'whiteness'—the qualities you think you have—are they merely surface appearances or rooted in a deeper transformation?
?
This proof shows that a specific process (cooking) produces a real change. In your own spiritual life, are you merely 'grinding'—going through the motions—without allowing the heat of sincere effort to transform your character? What would it take for you to undergo a genuine qualitative change?
?
The objection that the difference is just a matter of degree is answered by noting the unique effect of cooking. When you face similar objections in your faith—that your actions are just 'more of the same'—do you recognize the unique transformative power of certain practices? What one practice could you approach with the intention of real 'cooking' rather than mere 'grinding'?

Ibn Sina's Admission and the Charge of Sophistry
Ibn Sina's admission and the initial sophistry charge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of Ibn Sina's admission regarding whiteness in physical examples and how it led to the charge of sophistry against him.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina's admission that examples contain no true whiteness seems to deny the senses.

This passage presents the core of the debate: Ibn Sina's conclusion that whiteness in the physical world is not light, but he admits that the examples commonly cited (like foam) lack real whiteness, only containing reflected light. This admission appears to contradict sensory experience and invites the charge of sophistry. As you read, note how the author of the Mawāqif frames the issue and introduces the reaction of later theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains a statement from Ibn Sina, then the author's comment, then a discussion.
2
Identify Ibn Sina's admission
Find where the author says Ibn Sina admitted that there is no whiteness in the examples. This is the key claim that leads to the sophistry charge.
The phrase 'اعترف بأن لا بياض فيما ذكروه من الأمثلة' is central.
3
Understand the sophistry charge
See why this admission is considered sophistry: if there is no whiteness, then our senses are wrong. Note also that the passage mentions Ibn Sina's own qualification about reflected light, which sets up the refutation.
The commentator hints that Ibn Sina actually said something else, but the charge remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا فقد بان بهذه الوجوه أن البياض بالحقيقة في الأشياء ليس بضوء ثم لسنا نمنع أن يكون للضوء المضيء تأثير في التبيض . قال المصنف : ( وإذ قد تقرر ذلك فإنه قد اعترف ) أي ابن سينا بأن لا بياض فيما ذكروه من (الأمثلة وهي زبد الماء وأخواته ويلزم السفسطة ) وارتفاع الأمان عن الحس بالكلية. وهاهنا بحث وهو أنه قد صرح فيما نقلناه كلامه بأن المحسوس في هذه الأمثلة أمر موجود هو الضوء المتعاكس وجعله بياضاً حادثاً بطريق مخصوص وقال : وأما أنه هل يكون بياض غير هذا فمما لم أعلم بعد امتناعه ووجوده وسيأتي لي كلام في هذا المعنى أشد استقصاء وأشار به إلى الوجوه الخمسة الدالة على أنّ البياض قد يحدث بطريق آخر فيظهر أن البياض لون مغاير للضوء المسمى في تلك الأمثلة بياضاً وليس في هذا سفسطة وارتفاع أمان، لكن الإمام الرازي كما هو دأبه يتصرف فيما ينقله عنه ليتسع له مجال الاعتراض عليه ويقلده في ذلك من يتبعه . فلذلك قال صاحب الكتاب : ( والحق منعه ) أي منع أن لا بياض فيما ذكروه من الأمثلة ) والقول بأنَّ ذلك ) أي اختلاط الهواء المضيء بالأجزاء الشفافة ( أحد أسباب حدوث البياض وإن لم يكن هناك مزاج يتبعه حدوث اللون
قوله : ( أفادهما مزاجاً إلخ) فيكون حدوث البياض بطريق الاستحالة . قوله : (وارتفاع الأمان إلخ) لأنه حكم بوجود البياض في الأمثلة المذكورة ولا بياض في الحقيقة فيكون متهما ولا شهادة لمتهم . قوله : ( وهو أنه قد صرح فيما نقلناه إلخ) من قوله : وكان أصل البياض هو الضوء الذي استحال ببعض الوجوه ومن قوله : أن البياض بالحقيقة في الأشياء ليس بضوء فإنه كالتصريح بأن البياض في الأمثلة المذكورة ضوء مستحيل . وقيل : المراد أنه صرح فيما نقلناه وإن لم يكن ذلك المصرح مذكوراً هاهنا ولا يخفى بعده . قوله : ( وجعله بياضاً حادثاً) حيث قال : لا أعلم حدوث البياض بطريق آخر، وقال أيضاً بحث المزاج: إنَّ كثيراً من الأعراض يعرضه أيضاً بسبب مخالطة غير مزاجية وذكر الأمثلة المذكورة. قوله : ( وليس في هذا سفسطة لأنه لم يقل بأنه لا بياض وأنه متخيل كما قاله القدماء بل أنه أمر موجود حدث بطريق انعكاس الضوء من الهواء على الأجزاء المشفة . قوله : ( وهو أنه قد صرح إلخ ) وإن لم يذكر المصرح به هاهنا .
Ibn Sina said: It has become clear through these aspects that whiteness in reality in things is not light, then we do not deny that luminous light has an effect in whitening. The author said: Since that has been established, he has admitted, i.e., Ibn Sina, that there is no whiteness in the examples they mentioned, i.e., foam of water and its like, and sophistry follows and the loss of trust in the senses entirely. And here there is a discussion: he explicitly stated in what we transmitted of his words that the sensed thing in these examples is an existing thing, which is the reflected light, and he made it a whiteness occurring in a specific way, and he said: As for whether there is whiteness other than this, I have not yet known its impossibility or existence, and there will come from me speech on this meaning with greater investigation, and he pointed by that to the five aspects that indicate that whiteness may occur in another way, so it appears that whiteness is a color different from the light called whiteness in those examples, and there is no sophistry or loss of trust in this; but Imam al-Razi, as is his custom, manipulates what he transmits from him to give himself room for objection, and those who follow him imitate him in that. Therefore the author of the book said: The truth is to deny it, i.e., to deny that there is no whiteness in the examples they mentioned, and to say that that, i.e., the mixture of illuminated air with transparent parts, is one of the causes of the occurrence of whiteness even if there is no mixture that is followed by the occurrence of color. He said: (He gave them a mixture etc.) so the occurrence of whiteness is by way of transformation. He said: (And the loss of trust etc.) because he judged that whiteness exists in the mentioned examples while in reality there is no whiteness, so he is accused and the testimony of an accused is not accepted. He said: (And it is that he explicitly stated in what we transmitted etc.) from his words: The origin of whiteness is light that has been transformed in some ways, and from his words: Whiteness in reality in things is not light, for that is as if an explicit statement that whiteness in the mentioned examples is transformed light. And it was said: The intended meaning is that he explicitly stated in what we transmitted, even if that explicit statement is not mentioned here, and its distance is not hidden. He said: (And he made it a whiteness occurring) when he said: I do not know the occurrence of whiteness in another way, and he also said in the discussion of mixture: Many accidents also occur to it due to non-mixture mixing, and he mentioned the mentioned examples. He said: (And there is no sophistry in this) because he did not say that there is no whiteness and that it is imaginary as the ancients said, rather it is an existing thing that occurred by way of reflection of light from the air onto the transparent parts. He said: (And it is that he explicitly stated etc.) even if the explicit statement is not mentioned here.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage deals with the charge of sophistry leveled against Ibn Sina. Ibn Sina had concluded from five proofs that whiteness is not light, but he admitted that the examples cited by his opponents (foam, etc.) do not contain real whiteness, only reflected light. This led some to accuse him of denying sensory evidence. The commentator defends Ibn Sina by pointing out that Ibn Sina did not say those examples are entirely without whiteness; rather, he considered the perceived whiteness to be a real phenomenon caused by reflected light, but he also left open the possibility of whiteness arising from other causes. Thus, there is no sophistry. The commentator further criticizes Imam al-Razi for misrepresenting Ibn Sina's position to create objections. The passage clarifies that the five proofs actually show that whiteness is a color distinct from light, and that the examples involve a real whiteness produced by a specific mechanism, not a denial of the senses.

Key Terms
السفسطة
البياض
الضوء
الانعكاس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Ibn Sina admit regarding the examples of foam and its like?
A) That whiteness is light.
B) That there is no whiteness in them.
C) That whiteness is imaginary.
D) That whiteness is reflected light.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's initial admission seems to deny whiteness in common examples, but he qualifies it as reflected light, leading to the sophistry charge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine you are engaged in a deep discussion about the nature of reality, and someone accuses you of denying what is obvious to everyone. How would you respond when your words are taken out of context? Are you prepared to clarify your position with humility, as Ibn Sina did, or do you let the accusation stand?
?
Consider a time when you were convinced of something based on your senses, only to have someone offer a deeper explanation that reinterpreted what you saw. Did you dismiss their explanation as sophistry, or did you pause to consider that your senses might not tell the whole truth? What does this lesson teach you about trusting your perception when someone challenges its validity?
?
Al-Razi is criticized for manipulating Ibn Sina's words to create objections. Reflect on your own habits: do you ever twist someone's argument to make it easier to refute, rather than engaging with what they actually said? How can you commit to intellectual honesty, even when you strongly disagree?

Refuting the Sophistry Charge: Whiteness as Reflected Light
The commentator's refutation and explanation of Ibn Sina's position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends Ibn Sina by clarifying that he considered the perceived whiteness as real reflected light, not imaginary, and that he left open the possibility of other causes.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina affirmed a real whiteness as reflected light, so no sophistry.

In this continuation, the commentator directly refutes the charge of sophistry. He points out that Ibn Sina explicitly stated that the whiteness in the examples is a real phenomenon—reflected light—and he did not deny whiteness altogether. Moreover, he acknowledges that whiteness might arise from other causes, such as mixture of illuminated air with transparent parts. This passage also criticizes Imam al-Razi for distorting Ibn Sina's words to create objections.

Guided Path
1
Examine the commentator's denial
The author says 'The truth is to deny it'—meaning to deny that there is no whiteness. Read this section to see how the commentator rejects the charge.
Look for the phrase 'الحق منعه' and what follows.
2
Understand the explanation of reflected light
The commentator explains that Ibn Sina considered the whiteness in the examples as transformed light, and that he explicitly said it is not imaginary. Identify the key passage where he says this.
The phrase 'لم يقل بأنه لا بياض وأنه متخيل كما قاله القدماء بل أنه أمر موجود' is crucial.
3
Note the criticism of al-Razi
The passage criticizes al-Razi for manipulating Ibn Sina's words. See how this criticism connects to the overall defense.
This shows a sectarian dimension in the debate.

?

The Philosophers' View on Light and Color Is Less Plausible
Commentator argues that philosophers' claim about light and color is less plausible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends his position on whiteness by showing that the philosophers' view on light and color leads to an unacceptable consequence.

Core Idea
The philosophers' view on light and color is more far-fetched than the commentator's view.

The commentator has previously argued that whiteness can occur through mixing with air without temperamental change. Here he anticipates an objection: his view might seem far-fetched. He turns the tables, arguing that the standard philosophical position on light and color is actually more problematic because it implies that colors cease to exist in darkness and cannot return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views
Read the passage and find the commentator's claim about whiteness and the philosophers' claim about light and color.
The commentator's view is about whiteness occurring in transparent parts by mixing with air.
2
Understand the consequence of the philosophers' view
Notice the logical implication: if light is a condition for colors, then colors disappear in darkness and would need to be re-created when light returns.
The philosophers deny the return of the non-existent, so this is a problem for them.
3
Reflect on the comparison
Consider why the commentator thinks his view is less far-fetched: his view does not lead to such a contradiction.
The argument is stronger by showing the opponent's position is worse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس ذلك الذي قلنا به أبعد مما يقوله الحكماء في كون الضوء شرطاً لحدوث الألوان كلها . إذ يلزم منه انتفاء الألوان في الظلمة وحدوثها عند وقوع الضوء على محالها فإذا أخرج المصباح مثلاً عن البيت المظلم انتفي ألوان الأشياء التي فيها وإذا أعيد صارت ملونة بأمثالها لاستحالة إعادة المعدوم عندهم. ولا شك أنَّ هذا أبعد من حدوث البياض في الأجزاء الشفافة بمخالطة الهواء من غير مزاج.
And that which we have said is not more far-fetched than what the philosophers say about light being a condition for the occurrence of all colors. For it follows from it that colors cease to exist in darkness and occur when light falls upon their loci. So if a lamp, for example, is taken out of a dark room, the colors of the things in it cease to exist, and if it is returned, they become colored with their likes, because returning a non-existent is impossible according to them. There is no doubt that this is more far-fetched than the occurrence of whiteness in transparent parts by mixing with air without temperament.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is an argument (dalil) by the commentator defending his position on whiteness. He argues that his claim—that whiteness can occur in transparent parts through mixing with air without a temperamental change—is not more far-fetched than the standard philosophical view that light is a condition for the occurrence of all colors. He then explains the problematic consequence of that philosophical view: it implies that colors disappear in darkness and reappear when light returns. Since the philosophers hold that a non-existent cannot return, this leads to an absurdity. Therefore, the commentator's own view is actually less problematic. The argument proceeds by comparing the two positions and showing that the philosophers' position has an unacceptable implication, making his own view more plausible.

Key Terms
الحكماء
الضوء شرطاً
انتفاء الألوان
الظلمة
استحالة إعادة المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the commentator's view is more far-fetched than the philosophers' view on light and color.
Correction: The commentator shows that the philosophers' view leads to an absurdity (colors cannot return after ceasing to exist), making his own view actually more plausible.

Checkpoint
What problematic consequence does the commentator attribute to the philosophers' view that light is a condition for all colors?
A) Colors become permanent and unchanging.
B) Colors disappear in darkness and cannot return because a non-existent cannot be restored.
C) Colors are independent of light.
D) Whiteness is the only color that exists.

Key Takeaway
The commentator's view on whiteness is actually less problematic than the philosophers' view on light and color because the latter implies an impossible return of non-existent colors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that seemed obvious but actually contained a hidden contradiction? As you examined this lesson, what belief of your own about how the world works might need rethinking? Commit to questioning one assumption you have today.
?
The commentator shows that a widely accepted philosophical view is actually less plausible than his own. Is there an area of your faith where you have uncritically accepted an argument because 'everyone says so'? Will you take the time to trace its logical consequences as this lesson does?
?
This argument hinges on the principle that returning a non-existent is impossible. In your own life, do you sometimes act as if you can 'bring back' a past state—like a sin you thought was gone or a relationship you damaged? How does the impossibility of returning what is truly lost call you to seek true renewal through Allah, not through your own efforts?

Light as a Condition for Color: Ibn Sina's Argument
Ibn Sina states that light is a condition for the existence of color and refutes the obstacle hypothesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that light is a necessary condition for the actual existence of color, and why darkness cannot be an obstacle to vision.

Core Idea
Light is what makes color exist; darkness is merely absence, not a barrier.

Ibn Sina presents a foundational argument about the relationship between light and color. He claims that color does not actually exist in darkness; rather, the colored object is merely prepared to receive color when light appears. To support this, he considers and refutes the alternative that dark air could be an obstacle. This lesson walks through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal English translation. Notice the logical flow: thesis, evidence, disjunction, refutation.
Pay special attention to the cave example.
2
Identify the main claim and the two possibilities
Ibn Sina claims light is a condition for color existence. He then says that not seeing color in darkness could be due to either color's absence or an obstacle (dark air). Identify these two options.
3
Examine the refutation of the obstacle hypothesis
Ibn Sina uses the cave example to show that dark air does not prevent sight. Consider why darkness itself cannot be an obstacle because it is a non-entity.
This refutation strengthens his thesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن سينا وكثير من الحكماء الضوء شرط وجود اللون في نفسه فاللون إنما يحدث في الجسم بالفعل عند حصول الضوء فيه وأنه غير موجود في الظلمة لفقدان شرط وجوده حينئذ بل الجسم في الظلمة مستعد لأن يحصل فيه عند الضوء اللون المعين فإنا لا نراه في الظلمة فذلك إما لعدمه في نفسه أو لوجود العائق وهو الهواء المظلم والثاني باطل لأنَّ الهواء المظلم غير مانع من الإبصار فإن الجالس في غار مظلم يرى من في الخارج إذا أوقد ناراً وقع عليه ضوؤها والهواء الذي بينهما مع كونه مظلماً لا يعوق عن رؤيته وكيف تكون الظلمة عائقة عن الرؤية مع كونها أمراً عدمياً.
Ibn Sina and many philosophers said: Light is a condition for the existence of color in itself. So color only occurs in the body in actuality when light occurs in it. And that color does not exist in darkness due to the absence of its condition at that time. Rather, the body in darkness is prepared for the specific color to occur in it from light. For we do not see it in darkness. So that is either due to its non-existence in itself or due to the existence of an obstacle, which is the dark air. The second is invalid because the dark air does not prevent sight. For the person sitting in a dark cave sees whoever is outside when he lights a fire whose light falls on him, and the air between them, despite being dark, does not hinder his vision. And how can darkness be an obstacle to vision while it is a non-existent thing?
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's view that light is a necessary condition for the actual existence of color. It argues that color does not exist in darkness, but the body is merely disposed to receive color when light appears. The proof is based on our inability to see color in darkness. The author then considers two possibilities for this lack of visibility: either color is absent in itself, or there is an obstacle (the dark air). The second hypothesis is refuted by the example of a person in a dark cave who can see an external person illuminated by a fire; the dark air between them does not prevent vision. Since darkness is a non-entity (privation), it cannot act as a real obstacle.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think darkness is a positive entity that blocks vision or prevents color from being seen.
Correction: Darkness is merely the absence of light; it cannot be an active obstacle. The cave example shows that when light is present, darkness does not hinder sight.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that the dark air is an obstacle to seeing color?
A) Because dark air is transparent and does not block light.
B) Because a person in a dark cave can see someone outside when light is present.
C) Because darkness is a positive entity that prevents color existence.
D) Because color does not need light to exist.

Key Takeaway
Light is a necessary condition for the actual existence of color; darkness is not an obstacle but the absence of light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life have you blamed an 'obstacle' (like darkness) for your failure to see or act, only to realize the real missing condition was something else—like knowledge, intention, or reliance on Allah? Will you seek that condition today?
?
The person in the dark cave could see instantly when fire was lit. What is the 'fire' that could illuminate your own spiritual darkness? What is one step you can take this week to bring that light into your heart?
?
Ibn Sina shows that darkness is not an active barrier—it is just absence. Is there an area of obedience or worship where you treat your own laziness or ignorance as a positive barrier instead of a missing condition you could restore? Will you stop treating it as a wall and start seeking the condition?

Ibn al-Haytham's Argument from Gradation of Light and Color
Ibn al-Haytham argues that light is a condition for color existence based on proportional intensity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Haytham's argument that light is a condition for color existence based on proportional intensity, and why the argument falls short of being a demonstrative proof.

Core Idea
Gradation shows correlation, but inference is not proof.

Ibn al-Haytham attempts to prove that light is a condition for color by observing that colors weaken with light. This lesson unpacks his argument and reveals its reliance on inference (hads), which prevents it from being a certain proof. We'll also consider a possible objection that a layer of color might exist only in darkness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical steps Ibn al-Haytham uses.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the observation
Notice the observation: colors weaken as light weakens. This is the empirical starting point.
3
Trace the argument
Follow the reasoning: each layer of light conditions a layer of color; when all light layers go, all color layers go. Then he concludes that absolute color is negated in darkness.
Ask yourself: does the conclusion follow necessarily?
4
Recognize the flaw
Ibn al-Haytham admits his conclusion requires inference (hads). He also concedes the possibility of an unseen color layer in darkness.
Why does inference make it less than a proof?
5
Consider the objection
The passage ends with a possible objection: what differs by degrees (colors) might not be solely due to light. Reflect on how this weakens the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن الهيثم مستدلاً على أنَّ الضوء شرط لوجود اللون إنا نرى الألوان تضعف بحسب ضعف الضوء فكلما كان الضوء أقوى كان اللون أشده وكلما كان أضعف كان أضعف فكل طبقة من الضوء شرط لطبقة من اللون لانتفاء الثانية بانتفاء الأولى فإذا انتفي طبقات الأضواء كلها انتفى أيضاً طبقات الألوان بأسرها وهذا يوجب أنَّ هذه الألوان التي هي في ضمن هذه الطبقات تنتفي في الظلمة لانتفاء شروطها التي هي طبقات الأضواء فينتفي اللون المطلق أيضاً لأنَّ العام لا يوجد إلا في ضمن الخاص ولما احتمل أن يكون للون طبقة توجد في الظلمة فقط ولا يحس بها فيوجد للون المطلق في ضمنها قال ويحدس منه انتفاء اللون مطلقاً فاعترف بأن ما ذكره محتاج إلى الحدس فلا يكون حجة على الغير مع أن لقائل أن يقول المختلف بحسب مراتب.
Ibn al-Haytham said, as a proof that light is a condition for the existence of color: We see colors weaken according to the weakness of light. So whenever the light is stronger, the color is more intense, and whenever it is weaker, it is weaker. So each layer of light is a condition for a layer of color, because the second is negated by the negation of the first. So when all layers of light are negated, all layers of color are also negated entirely. This requires that these colors that are within these layers are negated in darkness due to the negation of their conditions that are the layers of light, so the absolute color is also negated, because the universal does not exist except within the particular. And when it was possible that color has a layer that exists only in darkness and is not sensed, so the absolute color exists within it, he said: 'And it is inferred from it the negation of color absolutely.' So he admitted that what he mentioned needs inference, so it is not a proof for others, although one could say: 'What differs according to degrees...'
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn al-Haytham's argument that light is a condition for the existence of color. The argument is based on the observation that color intensity varies proportionally with light intensity. Since each level of light corresponds to a specific level of color, and if all light levels are removed, all color levels are removed, then color ceases to exist in darkness. The author then points out a flaw: there could be a layer of color that exists only in darkness and is imperceptible, so the absolute color might still exist within that layer. Ibn al-Haytham concedes that his conclusion relies on inference (hads), so it is not a conclusive proof. Moreover, an objector could argue that differences in color intensity might not be solely due to light levels but perhaps something else.

Key Terms
طبقات
تضعف
مطلق
خاص
الحدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The gradation argument is a certain proof that light is a condition for all colors.
Correction: The argument relies on inference (hads) and does not account for possible imperceptible colors in darkness, so it is not demonstrative.

Checkpoint
Why does Ibn al-Haytham's argument not constitute a demonstrative proof?
A) The observation that colors weaken is inaccurate.
B) It relies on inference (hads) and there could be an imperceptible color layer in darkness.
C) Light and color are not related.
D) The universal exists without the particular.

Key Takeaway
The gradation argument shows correlation between light and color intensity but fails as a certain proof because it relies on inference and leaves open the possibility of imperceptible colors in darkness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn al-Haytham observed a correlation and concluded a necessary condition. But he admitted it was inference, not proof. What conviction do you hold that you treat as certain but is actually based on inference from limited observation? Will you examine it today?
?
The possibility of imperceptible color in darkness—a hidden possibility—undermines the argument. In your own life, are there hidden possibilities you ignore because they contradict your comfortable conclusions? What would change if you took them seriously?
?
Ibn al-Haytham's admission that his reasoning 'is not a proof for others' shows intellectual honesty. Do you have the courage to admit when your own arguments fall short? How might admitting uncertainty strengthen your faith rather than weaken it?

Light as a Condition for Vision
Al-Razi's distinction between condition for vision vs. existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's argument that light is a condition for seeing color, not for its existence.

Core Idea
Light conditions vision, not the existence of color.

Imam al-Razi opens the debate by distinguishing between color's existence and our perception of it. He argues that darkness prevents vision, not color itself. The cave example illustrates that light is needed for seeing, not for the color to be present. This lesson sets the stage for the deeper discussion on causation.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's statement
Read the Arabic passage where al-Razi states that light is a condition for seeing color, not for its existence. Then read the translation.
Notice how he distinguishes between two types of absence: absence of vision vs. absence of color.
2
Examine the cave example
Al-Razi gives the example of a person in a cave. Understand why the outside person sees the one inside only when light enters, and why the inside person sees the outside only if illuminated.
This shows that light is needed for sight, not for the color of objects.
3
Identify the logical structure
Al-Razi argues that the non-occurrence of vision in darkness is due to the absence of the condition (light), not an obstacle or the non-existence of color. Identify this tripartite division.
This is a key logical move: denying the exclusive division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - القسم الأول : المقصد الثاني : الضوء شرط وجود اللون
الإمام الرازي أنه أي الضوء شرط لرؤيته لا لوجوده فإنَّ رؤيته زائدة على ذاته والمتحقق المتيقن عدم رؤيته في الظلمة وأما عدمه في نفسه فلا. فانتفاء الرؤية في الظلمة لعدم شرط الرؤية لا لوجود العائق عنها ولا لعدم اللون في نفسه والجالس في الغار إنما لا يراه الخارج عنه لعدم إحاطة الضوء المحيط بالمرئي ولذلك يرى الجالس الخارج المستضيء بالنار قال ابن الهيثم مستدلاً على أنَّ الضوء شرط لوجود اللون إنا نرى الألوان تضعف بحسب ضعف الضوء فكلما كان الضوء أقوى كان اللون أشده، كلما كان أضعف كان أضعف فكل طبقة من الضوء شرط لطبقة من اللون لانتفاء الثانية بانتفاء الأولى فإذا انتفي طبقات الأضواء كلها انتفى أيضاً طبقات الألوان بأسرها وهذا يوجب أنَّ هذه الألوان التي هي في ضمن هذه الطبقات تنتفي في الظلمة لانتفاء شروطها التي هي طبقات الأضواء فينتفي اللون المطلق أيضاً، لأنَّ العام لا يوجد إلا في ضمن الخاص ولما احتمل أن يكون للون طبقة توجد في الظلمة فقط ولا يحس بها فيوجد للون المطلق في ضمنها . قال : ويحدس منه انتفاء اللون مطلقاً فاعترف بأن ما ذكره محتاج إلى الحدس فلا يكون حجة على الغير مع أن لقائل أن يقول : المختلف بحسب مراتب على عدم كونه عائقاً فإن تم دل على ثبوت المدعى من غير توسط كونها عدمية وإلا فلا. .. نعم لو أثبت كونها عدمية بدليل آخر لكان عدميتها وجهاً آخر لعدم عائقيتها .
قوله : فانتفاء الرؤية إلخ إشارة إلى أن خلاصة الجواب منع الحصر المستفاد من قوله إما لعدمه في نفسه أو لوجود العائق . فقوله : والجالس في الغار إلخ . زائد على الجواب للاستظهار . قوله : لانتفاء الثانية إلخ فيه أنَّ اللازم مما ذكر انتفاء الثانية مع انتفاء الأولى وهو يستلزم التوقف حتى يثبت الشرطية .
احتمال أن يكون العائق الظلمة المحيطة بالمرئي كما سيجيء من المصنف إلا بناء على أنَّ إثبات عدميتها لا يتم نظراً إلى ذلك الاحتمال .
قوله : وهو مختار الإمام الرازي قال في المباحث المشرقية : الأقرب أن كون الشيء ملوناً بالفعل لا يتوقف على كونه مضيئاً بالفعل، لأن قابلية الجسم للضوء موقوفة على كونه ملوناً ولذلك قال : الشفاف لا يكون قابلا للضوء والنور بالفعل فإذا كان قابلية الجسم للضوء موقوفة على وجود اللون فلو توقف وجود اللون على وجود الضوء بالفعل لزم الدور وسيجيء جوابه في المقصد الثالث من القسم الثاني . قوله : ولما احتمل أن يكون وأيضاً احتمل أن يقال : إِنَّ انتفاء اللون المحسوس مع مرتبة من مراتب الضوء عند انتفائها ليس لانتفائها بل لأمر آخر مجهول لنا . قوله : مع أن لقائل أن يقول : وأيضاً الواصل إلى الحس المشترك تارة هو اللون مع ضوء الأضواء هو الرؤية المشروطة بها لا اللون في نفسه فيكون للرؤية مراتب جلاء وخفاء بحسب شدة الأضواء وضعفها مع كون المرئي الذي هو للون باقياً على حالة واحدة وأنت تعرف أنَّ مذهب أهل الحق أنَّ الرؤية سواء كانت متعلقة بالألوان أو بغيرها أمر يخلقه الله في الحي على وفق مشيئته ولا يشترط بضوء ولا مقابلة ولا غيرهما من الشرائط التي اعتبرها الحكماء والمعتزلة على ما سيأتي في مباحث رؤية الله تعالى وإنما لا نتعرض لأمثاله للاعتماد على معرفتك بها في موضعها فعليك برعاية قواعد أهل الحق في جميع المباحث وإن لم نصرح بها .
The third observation - First section - Second objective: Light is a condition for the existence of color. Imam al-Razi [stated] that it, i.e., light, is a condition for seeing it (color), not for its existence, for its seeing is additional to its essence, and what is verified and certain is the non-seeing of it in darkness, but as for its non-existence in itself, [that is not certain]. So the non-occurrence of vision in darkness is due to the absence of the condition of vision, not because of the existence of an obstacle to it, nor because of the non-existence of color in itself. And the one sitting in the cave is not seen by the person outside due to the lack of encompassing light surrounding the visible object, and therefore the sitting person sees the outside person illuminated by fire. Ibn al-Haytham said, arguing that light is a condition for the existence of color: We see colors weaken according to the weakness of light; the stronger the light, the more intense the color, and the weaker the light, the weaker the color. So each level of light is a condition for a level of color, because the second level is eliminated by the elimination of the first. So if all levels of light are eliminated, the levels of color are also entirely eliminated. And this necessitates that these colors that are within these levels are eliminated in darkness due to the elimination of their conditions, which are the levels of light, so absolute color is also eliminated, because the universal does not exist except within the particular. And since it is possible that color has a level that exists in darkness only and is not sensed, so absolute color exists within it, he said: And one conjectures from it the elimination of color absolutely. So he admitted that what he mentioned requires conjecture, so it is not a proof for others. Moreover, someone may say: The variation according to degrees... upon its not being an obstacle. If it is established, it proves the claim without mediation of its being privative, otherwise not. Yes, if its being privative were established by another evidence, its privativity would be another aspect for its not being an obstacle. His statement 'so the non-occurrence of vision, etc.' is an indication that the summary of the response is the denial of the exclusive division [implied] by his statement 'either due to its non-existence in itself or due to the existence of an obstacle.' So his statement 'and the one sitting in the cave, etc.' is additional to the response for corroboration. His statement 'because the second is eliminated with the elimination of the first' contains [the point] that what is necessary from what was mentioned is the elimination of the second with the elimination of the first, and this requires waiting until the conditionality is proven. A possibility that the obstacle is the darkness surrounding the visible object, as will come from the author, except based on the fact that proving its privativity is not complete considering that possibility. His statement: 'And it is the choice of Imam al-Razi.' He said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: The closest [view] is that a thing's being actually colored does not depend on its being actually illuminated, because the body's receptivity to light depends on its being colored. Therefore he said: The transparent is not receptive to light and illumination actually. So if the body's receptivity to light depends on the existence of color, then if the existence of color depended on the existence of light actually, a vicious circle would result. Its answer will come in the third objective of the second section. His statement: 'And since it is possible that...' Also it is possible to say: Indeed, the elimination of the sensed color with a degree of light when it is eliminated is not due to its elimination but due to another unknown cause. His statement: 'Moreover, someone may say:' Also, what reaches the common sense is sometimes the color with light... The light is the vision conditioned by it, not the color itself, so vision has degrees of clarity and dimness according to the intensity and weakness of lights, while the visible object, which is color, remains in a single state. And you know that the doctrine of the People of Truth is that vision, whether related to colors or other things, is a thing that God creates in the living being according to His will, and it is not conditioned by light, nor by facing, nor by other conditions that the philosophers and Mu'tazila considered, as will come in the discussions on seeing God. And we do not engage in such matters because we rely on your knowledge of them in their place. So upon you is to observe the principles of the People of Truth in all investigations even if we do not explicitly state them.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents the debate on whether light is a condition for the existence of color or only for its perception. It opens with Imam al-Razi's view that light is a condition for seeing color, not for its existence. He supports this with the example of the person in a cave: the outside person sees the one in the cave when light falls on the cave, but the person in the cave does not see the outside unless that outside is illuminated. This shows that light is needed for vision, not for color's existence. Then Ibn al-Haytham argues for the opposite: colors weaken with weakening light, so light must be a condition for existence. He reasons that if all light levels are gone, all color levels vanish, and since the universal exists only in particulars, absolute color vanishes in darkness. However, the commentator points out that Ibn al-Haytham admits his argument relies on conjecture, so it is not a proof. The commentary then analyzes the logic: the denial of the exclusive division (either non-existence of color or an obstacle) is the key response. It also notes that darkness might be an obstacle, but proving it is privative is difficult. Next, al-Razi's argument from al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya is cited: if color depended on light, a circularity would result because a body's receptivity to light depends on its being colored. The passage concludes by affirming the Asharite doctrine that vision is created by God without any conditions like light or facing. The reader is reminded to adhere to this principle in all investigations.

Key Terms

الرؤية
شرط
العائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when an object is not seen in darkness, it must mean the color is not there.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that non-vision does not imply non-existence; darkness simply lacks the condition for seeing.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, why do we not see colors in darkness?
A) Because colors cease to exist in darkness.
B) Because an obstacle blocks vision.
C) Because the condition for vision (light) is absent.
D) Because the eye is not functioning.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi establishes that light is a condition for seeing color, not for color's existence, using the cave example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a period of spiritual darkness where you do not perceive Allah's guidance, do you assume His guidance has ceased to exist, or do you recognize that the condition for perceiving it (e.g., sincere seeking, humility) may be absent? What condition might you be missing right now?
?
Think of a time you concluded that something was false simply because you could not see evidence for it. How does al-Razi's distinction between non-perception and non-existence challenge that conclusion? What would it mean to apply this principle to your doubts about the unseen?
?
In your daily life, you often rely on your senses to judge reality. How does this lesson expose the risk of confusing 'I do not see it' with 'it is not there'? What specific step can you take this week to avoid that confusion in matters of faith?

Ibn al-Haytham's Argument for Light as Existence Condition
Ibn al-Haytham's argument for light conditioning color's existence and its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Haytham's reasoning that light is a condition for color's existence and the critique that his argument relies on conjecture.

Core Idea
Colors weaken with light, so light conditions their existence.

Ibn al-Haytham challenges al-Razi's view by observing that colors appear more intense in stronger light and fade in weaker light. He argues that this correlation shows light is a condition for the very existence of color, not just its perception. However, the commentator points out that Ibn al-Haytham admits his argument is based on conjecture, which weakens its force.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn al-Haytham's argument
Read the Arabic passage where Ibn al-Haytham argues from the weakening of colors with light. Note how he uses correlation.
Look for the phrase 'إنا نرى الألوان تضعف بحسب ضعف الضوء'.
2
Understand the logical structure
Ibn al-Haytham claims that each level of light corresponds to a level of color, and when all light is gone, all color levels vanish, implying absolute color vanishes.
He uses the principle that the universal exists only in particulars.
3
Identify the critique
The commentator notes that Ibn al-Haytham says 'يحدس منه' (one conjectures), admitting his argument is not certain.
Also, there is a possibility that color has an unseen level in darkness.
4
Reflect on the mutual dependence
Consider the counterarguments: maybe the observed correlation is due to perception, not existence; maybe there is an unknown cause.
This prepares for the next lesson on circularity.
